"name": "Introduction", "content": "\\\\label{sec:intro}Large language models learn surprising capabilities from pre-training on large datasets\\\\citep{gpt2FewShotLearners, chowdhery2023palm, touvron2023llama}.\\nWhile these capabilities lead to impressive achievements, they also include unwanted behaviors that can be found in large-scale web data, such as toxicity and bias\\\\citep{sheng-etal-2019-woman, gehman-etal-2020-realtoxicityprompts}.\\nAs a result, researchers have developed alignment algorithms to reduce undesirable behaviors, which often use reinforcement learning with human preferences (RLHF).\\nFor instance, proximal policy optimization (PPO,\\\\citealt{schulman2017proximal}) fits a reward model on human preference data, which is then used to fine-tune a language model, while direct preference optimization (DPO,\\\\citealt{rafailov2023direct}) by-passes the reward model and derives reward signals directly from pairwise preference data.\\n\\nWhile such algorithms can suppress undesirable behavior, our understanding of the mechanisms by which the undesirable behavior is suppressed is limited.\\nFurthermore, researchers have demonstrated that such alignments can be surprisingly easily undone\\\\citep{wallace-etal-2019-universal, zou2023universal, wei2023jailbroken, carlini2023are}.\\nWhile prior work hypothesize why jailbreaks are possible through empirical studies~\\\\citep{wei2023jailbroken}, in this work we provide a mechanistic explanation for such phenomena.\\n\\n\\nGiven the above limitations, in this work we study the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms alter a model\'s behavior.\\nResearchers have demonstrated that a deep enough understanding of a model\'s inner representations allows us to interpret how it makes decisions.\\nFor instance, various concepts such as world models, truthfulness, or even task-specific features have highly interpretable and controllable representations\\\\citep{li2023inference, todd2023function, nanda2023emergent}.\\nMotivated by such findings, we study how the representation space of language models change by comparing it before and after an alignment algorithm is applied.\\nOur work relates to that of\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}, which studies how the capabilities of a language model changes after fine-tuning on synthetic tasks.\\nUnlike this previous work, we study the change in mechanisms from a RLHF algorithm on a natural language setting.\\n\\nWe consider DPO and toxicity as a case-study of RLHF alignment algorithms.\\nWe first study how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2-medium (henceforth GPT2).\\nWe then apply DPO using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset that consists of toxic and nontoxic samples.\\nLastly, we study the mechanisms by which toxicity is no longer generated after DPO, and how those mechanisms can fail.\\n\\n\\nOur work is organized as follows: in and elicited in GPT2-medium (henceforth GPT2).\\nWe then apply DPO using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset that consists of toxic and nontoxic samples.\\nLastly, we study the mechanisms by which toxicity is no longer generated after DPO, and how those mechanisms can fail.\\n\\n\\nOur work is organized as follows: in Section~\\\\ref{sec:preliminaries}we provide the necessary preliminaries relevant to our work.\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}, we demonstrate how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2.\\nWe find multiple vectors in multilayer perceptron (MLP) blocks that promote toxicity.\\nWe apply singular value decomposition (SVD) to these toxic vectors to find vectors that represent specific dimensions of toxicity in the model.\\nTo validate the role language models to reduce toxicity, using a carefully crafted pairwise toxicity dataset, produced by using PPLM~\\\\citep{dathathri2019plug}to generate paired toxic and non-toxic samples.\\n\\n\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:post_dpo}, we demonstrate how toxicity is no longer elicited after DPO.\\nNamely, we show that every parameter is minimally shifted, including the toxic vectors.\\nHowever, such minimal changes in weights allow the model to avert the triggering of toxic vectors.\\nPut differently, DPO\\\\emph{does not remove}the capability of generating toxic outputs, but learns an``offset\'\', distributed amongst its layers, to``bypass\'\'the regions that elicit toxicity.\\nBased on this understanding, we demonstrate the ease of re-activating these vectors to generate toxic "Preliminaries", "content": "\\\\label{sec:preliminaries}In this section we provide background and notations, much of which is borrowed from\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}.\\\\paragraph{Transformers, MLPs.}Transformer-based language models typically consists of embedding and unembedding layers$E, U \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{|\\\\mathcal{V}|\\\\times d}$with a series of$L$transformer layers in-between\\\\citep{attentionIsAllYouNeed}.\\nEach layer$l$consists of attention heads and a multilayer perception (MLP) layer.\\n\\nGiven an input sequence${\\\\mathbf{w} = \\\\langle w_0, ..., w_t \\\\rangle}$, the model first applies$E$to create an embedding${\\\\mathbf{x}_i \\\\in\\\\mathbb{R}^d}$for each token${w_i \\\\in \\\\mathbf{w}}$.\\nWe call${\\\\mathbf{x}_i}$the residual stream.\\n\\nThe residual stream is then updated by attention heads and MLP blocks from subsequent layers (bias terms omitted):$$\\n    \\\\mathbf{x^{\\\\ell+1}_i} = x^\\\\ell_i + \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(x^\\\\ell_i + \\\\texttt{Att}^\\\\ell(x^\\\\ell_i))\\n$$When needed, we specify the intermittent residual stream at layer$\\\\ell$(after the attention head, before the MLP) as$\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell\\\\_mid}$.\\nPer\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}, the updates to the residual stream from each MLP block can be further decomposed.\\nNamely, MLP blocks consist of two linear transformations, with point-wise activations$\\\\sigma$in-between:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp}\\n    \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(\\\\mathbf{x}^\\\\ell) = \\\\sigma\\\\left(W_K^\\\\ell\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\right) W_V^\\\\ell,\\n\\\\end{align}where$W_K^\\\\ell, W_V^\\\\ell \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d_{mlp} \\\\times d}$.\\nWe notate the$i$-th row in$W_K$as$\\\\text{MLP.}\\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}$and refer to them as key-vectors, and the$i$-th column in$W_V$,$\\\\text{MLP.}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}$, as value-vectors (we sometimes omit``$\\\\text{MLP}$\'\'and just use$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}, \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$).\\n\\nEquation~\\\\eqref{eq:mlp}indicates that\\\\emph{the output of MLP blocks is the sum of its value vectors $\\\\mathbf{v}_i$, each scaled by a coefficient value $m_i^\\\\ell$}, where$\\\\mathbf{m}^\\\\ell := \\\\sigma\\\\left(W_K^\\\\ell \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\right) \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d_{mlp}}$:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp_as_sum_of_value_vectors}\\n  \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}) = \\\\sum_{i=1}^{d_{mlp}} \\\\sigma(\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}) \\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} = \\\\sum_{i=1}^{d_{mlp}} m_i^{\\\\ell} \\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}.\\n\\\\end{align}Put differently, the MLP block writes to the residual stream$d_{mlp}$times, once for each value vector.\\nWe call each of these updates a\\\\emph{sub-update}.\\n\\nAll of our experiments are conducted with GPT2-medium, which has$L = 24$,$d = 1024$, and$d_{mlp} = 4096$.\\\\paragraph{Interpreting Value Vectors in Vocabulary Space.}\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that for each sub-update, each value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$either promotes or suppresses the likelihood of a token$w$from being generated:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:value_vecs_promote}\\n  p&\\\\big(w \\\\;|\\\\; \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}, E\\\\big) &\\\\propto \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)} \\\\cdot \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}\\n  \\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}where$\\\\mathbf{e}_w$is the embedding of$w$.\\nThis indicates that when${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} > 0}$, the likelihood of$w$increases, while${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} < 0}$decreases the likelihood.\\\\footnote{See Appendix for derivation.}Further note that this dot product can be further decomposed.\\nNamely,$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is a``static\'\'value that does not depend on the input: only when$\\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is scaled by$m_i$(which is determined by the its corresponding key vector,$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell$, and the residual stream$\\\\textbf{x}$) do we see the impact of the ', 'in Vocabulary Space.}\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that for each sub-update, each value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$either promotes or suppresses the likelihood of a token$w$from being generated:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:value_vecs_promote}\\n  p&\\\\big(w \\\\;|\\\\; \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}, E\\\\big) &\\\\propto \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)} \\\\cdot \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}\\n  \\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}where$\\\\mathbf{e}_w$is the embedding of$w$.\\nThis indicates that when${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} > 0}$, the likelihood of$w$increases, while${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} < 0}$decreases the likelihood.\\\\footnote{See Appendix for derivation.}Further note that this dot product can be further decomposed.\\nNamely,$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is a``static\'\'value that does not depend on the input: only when$\\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is scaled by$m_i$(which is determined by the its corresponding key vector,$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell$, and the residual stream$\\\\textbf{x}$) do we see the impact of the input towards the likelihood of$w$.\\n\\nThus the projection${\\\\mathbf{r}_i^\\\\ell = E \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{|\\\\mathcal{V}|}}$induces a ranking of tokens that get promoted by value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$, in which tokens with the highest dot products$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i$are promoted most by value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$.\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}we show value vectors that "Extracting Toxic Vectors", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:extracting_toxic_vectors}\\\\paragraph{Toxicity Probe Vector.}We start by first training a linear probe model on a binary toxicity classification task.\\nNamely, we use the Jigsaw toxic comment classification dataset\\\\citep{jigsaw-toxic-comment-classification-challenge}, which consists of 561,808 comments, each of which is labeled as toxic or non-toxic.\\nWe use a 90:10 split for training and validation.\\nWe train our probe model,\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}, on the residual stream in the last layer, averaged across all timesteps ($\\\\mathbf{\\\\bar{x}}^{L-1}$):\\\\begin{align}\\nP(\\\\text{Toxic} | \\\\mathbf{\\\\bar{x}}^{L-1}) = \\\\text{softmax}(W_{\\\\text{Toxic}}\\\\mathbf{\\\\bar{x}}^{L-1}), W_{\\\\text{Toxic}} \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d}\\n\\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}Our probe vector achieves an accuracy of 94\\\\%on the validation split.\\nWe view our toxic probe vector\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}as an aggregate of all the relevant signals in the language model to classify an input as toxic.\\\\paragraph{Toxic Vectors in MLP Blocks.}Given our probe vector\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}, we can use it to find weights within the language model that promote toxicity.\\nNamely,\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that value vectors promote tokens at a concept-level. \\nGiven this, we search for value vectors that promote toxicity, by checking for all value vectors with the highest cosine similarity with\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}.\\nWe find that "content": "\\\\label{subsec:dpo_background}DPO relies on pairwise preference data, in which given a prompt, we have a preferred (positive) continuation and a non-preferred (negative) continuation.\\nGiven each preference pair, the algorithm promotes the likelihood of the positive sample, while suppressing the likelihood of the negative sample, using the following loss term:\\\\begin{equation}\\n\\\\begin{aligned}\\n    &\\\\mathcal{L}_\\\\text{DPO} = -\\\\mathbb{E}\\\\left[\\\\log \\\\sigma \\\\left(\\\\beta \\\\log P - \\\\beta \\\\log N\\\\right)\\\\right] \\\\nonumber, \\\\\\\\\\n    &P = \\\\frac{\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}(y_+\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}{\\\\pi_{ref}(y_+\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}, N = \\\\frac{\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}(y_-\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}{\\\\pi_{ref}(y_-\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})},\\n\\\\end{aligned}\\n\\\\end{equation}where$y_+$and$y_-$are preferred (nontoxic) and non-preferred (toxic) continuations of$\\\\mathbf{w}$,$\\\\pi_{ref}$is the frozen weights of the original language model, and$\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}$is the weights of the language model being updated (See\\\\citet{rafailov2023direct}for details).\\nThe algorithm promotes the likelihood of$P$, while suppressing the likelihood of$N$.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "4.2", "name": "Constructing Pairwise Toxic Data", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:construct_dpo_data}We build our pairwise toxicity dataset using PPLM\\\\citep{dathathri2019plug}.\\nPPLM is an attribute-controlled language generation technique, which attaches a simple linear attribute classification layer,$p(a|\\\\mathbf{w})$onto a language model to guide its generation.\\nDuring generation, PPLM uses the attribute classifier to compute the gradients that increases the likelihood of the language model\'s output to contain the desired attribute$a$, and shifts the activations in such direction (See\\\\citet{dathathri2019plug}for details):\\\\useshortskip$$p(y\\\\mid a) \\\\propto p(y)p(a \\\\mid y)$$To generate pairwise preference data, we use sentences from Wikitext-2\\\\citep{merity2016pointer}as prompts.\\nFor each prompt, we generate a positive sample using greedy sampling with GPT2, while using PPLM to generate negative (toxic) samples.\\nWe use our toxic probe\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}as our attribute classifier to guide towards toxic outputs.\\nWe create 24,576 pairs of toxic and nontoxic continuations.\\\\footnote{We release this data to enable further studies.}We train until validation loss converges with a patience value of 10, which occurs after approximately 6,000 sample pairs.\\nAppendix~\\\\ref{sec:hyperparams}has details for DPO and PPLM hyperparameters.\\\\begin{figure}\\n\\\\vskip "\\\\label{subsec:toxic_vectors_after_dpo}Of the toxic vectors described in Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}, note that\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}are actual weights of the model. \\nThus we inspect how these vectors change after DPO.%We notate the toxic MLP vectors in the original language model as \\\\mlpgpt{} and those from the DPO model as \\\\mlpdpo{}.Interestingly, we find that every parameter in GPT2 and\\\\dpo{}has barely changed, including token embeddings, MLP blocks, and attention heads.\\nEvery parameter in GPT2 and its counterpart in\\\\dpo{}has a cosine similarity score greater than 0.99 and on average a norm difference less than 1e-5.\\\\footnote{The unembedding layer is the only exception, where the norm difference is less than 1e-3.}This applies for$\\\\text{MLP}.\\\\mathbf{k}_{\\\\text{Toxic}}$and\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}as well--toxic MLP vectors\\\\textbf{do not change}from DPO.\\n\\nPut differently, although toxicity is reduced by DPO, the ability to elicit toxicity with these value vectors still remain.\\nSo how is it that\\\\dpo{}averts toxic outputs?\\nThough its parameters have barely moved, below we show that their collective movement is enough to avoid toxic outputs.\\\\begin{figure}\\n\\\\vskip "\\\\label{subsec:avoiding_toxic_regions}In simplest terms, we observe a drop in activations for the toxic vectors\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}in\\\\dpo{}.\\nNamely, using the same 1,199 prompts from\\\\textsc{RealToxicityPrompts}, we generate 20 tokens and measure the mean activations$m_i$, or$\\\\sigma(\\\\mathbf{x}^\\\\ell \\\\cdot \\\\text{MLP.}\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell)$, of our\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}vectors.\\nFigure~\\\\ref{fig:activation_drops}shows 5 examples of the top\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}vectors.\\n\\nInspired by\\\\citet{balestriero2023characterizing}, we visualize this drop in activations with what we call``MLP activation regions\'\'.\\nAn activation region of a key vector is simply a\\\\emph{subspace}within the model\'s hidden space in which its vectors have high dot products to activate its corresponding value vector:\\\\begin{equation}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp_region}\\n    \\\\gamma(\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell) := \\\\{\\\\mathbf{g} | \\\\mathbf{g} \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^d, \\\\sigma(\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{g}) > 0\\\\},\\n\\\\end{equation}where$\\\\sigma$is a non-linear activation. \\nPut differently, for all key-vector regions that the residual stream``passes through\'\', their corresponding value-vectors are activated, scaled, and added into the residual stream.\\n\\nWe view the drop in activations as a shift in\\\\dpo{}\'s \\\\mathbf{g}) > 0\\\\},\\n\\\\end{equation}where$\\\\sigma$is a non-linear activation. \\nPut differently, for all key-vector regions that the residual stream``passes through'', their corresponding Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_jailbreak}We view our work as providing a mechanistic explanation for why aligned models can be undone or jailbroken--in our experiments, the regions that previously elicited toxic behavior does not change after DPO.\\nRather,\\\\dpo{}learns minimal changes spread across layers to avoid such regions and receive its reward.\\n\\nWith such knowledge, we conjecture that more robust alignment algorithms can be designed.\\nFor instance, can we eliminate undesirable regions, as opposed to bypassing them?\\nIn scenarios like ours, in which we can identify the weights that elicit undesirable outputs, what happens if we only updated those weights in isolation?\\nSimilarly, if DPO merely learned an offset that avoids toxic regions, can we replicate this behavior by only updating the bias terms?\\n\\nAlternatively, prior to deploying language models, perhaps we can add``suppression heads\'\'--layers that suppress undesirable behavior.\\nWhat would happen if we only updated late layers (or added layers) during alignment?\\n\\nLastly, can we characterize``jailbreak-ability\'\'or``unalign-ability\'\'of aligned models, without relying on test samples?\\n\\nWe leave these questions for future work.%\\\\subsection{Characterizing Robustness}%\\\\label{subsec:measuring_alignment_robustness}%If alignment robustness is the goal, how should we characterize robustness?%The answer may depend on the attack.%To the best of our knowledge, current literature lacks a taxonomy of adversarial cases.%For instance, whether an adversary only has access to an API, or to the weights of a model can lead to different attacks.%Our safety designs may depend largely on such characteristics.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "6.2", "name": "On the Role of KL-Divergence Regularization", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_kl_div}We hypothesize that the minimal changes distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not ', 'distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not entail a KL-divergence term.\\nPerhaps this allows the model to make drastic and localized changes, such as in late layers, as opposed to distributed, minimal changes.%\\\\subsection{On Generalizing Findings from Our Case Study}%\\\\label{subsec:generalizing_findings_from_case_study}%We note that our findings are from a case study, on a specific language model, task, and data set.%An obvious extension would be to study a larger model.%While in principle, we do not expect the "subsection": [{"id": "7.1", "name": "Alignment Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_alignment_algorithms}Numerous alignment algorithms have been proposed, and the choice of algorithm may largely depend on the type of data available.\\nPerhaps most commonly, human feedback data is used\\\\citep{stiennon2020learning, ouyang2022training, touvron2023llama}for methods such as PPO\\\\citep{schulman2017proximal}or DPO\\\\citep{rafailov2023direct}.\\nWhen labels for only undesirable behavior is available, algorithms like unlikelihood training\\\\citep{Welleck2020Neural}or Cringe\\\\citep{adolphs-etal-2023-cringe, xu2023some}can be used.\\nWe study DPO because it is easy to use and currently widely used.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.2", "name": "Mechanistic Interpretability", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_mech_interp}The goal of mechanistic interpretability is largely to reverse engineer model behaviors\\\\citep{olah2020zoom, elhage2021mathematical, geva-etal-2021-transformer}.\\nBy doing so, researchers have uncovered various interpretable and controllable representations, such as world models\\\\citep{li2023emergent, nanda2023emergent},``truthfulness\'\'\\\\citep{li2023inference}, knowledge\\\\citep{meng2022locating, hernandez2023linearity, burns2023discovering, geva2023dissecting}, linguistic properties\\\\citep{conneau-etal-2018-cram, tenney-etal-2019-bert}, or even tasks\\\\citep{ilharco2022editing, hendel-etal-2023-context, todd2023function}.\\n\\nRather than probing for specific representations, researchers have also characterized the representations of language models from a geometric perspective\\\\citep{park2023linear}.\\\\citet{balestriero2023characterizing}demonstrate a geometric characterization that can be used to extract feature representations that solve toxicity detection.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}study the mechanisms in which fine-tuning on synthetic tasks alters the model\'s capabilities.\\nWe study the effects of RLHF on a more realistic, natural language setting.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.3", "name": "Jailbreaking Aligned Models", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_jailbreak}Researchers demonstrated that aligned models can be surprisingly easily jailbroken\\\\citep{wallace-etal-2019-universal, zou2023universal, wei2023jailbroken, carlini2023are}.\\nSuch adversarial attacks typically involve searching for prompts that can elicit previously unlearned behaviors, or even personal information\\\\citep{nasr2023scalable}.\\\\citet{carlini2023are}show that multimodal models can also be jailbroken.\\\\citet{wei2023jailbroken}provide hypotheses, backed by empirical studies, as to why language models can be jailbroken.\\n\\nIn a similar vein to jailbreaks, numerous researchers have demonstrated that aligned models can easily be un-aligned\\\\citep{yang2023shadow, qi2023fine}, sometimes with as few as 100 fine-tuning examples.\\nWe view our work as adding a mechanistic understanding of such phenomena.", "subsection": []}]}', '{"id": "8", "name": "Conclusion", "content": "\\\\label{sec:conclusion}In this work we studied the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms unlearn a capability, taking DPO and toxicity as a case study.\\nFirst, we uncovered how toxicity is represented and elicited in a pre-trained language model.\\nWe find numerous vectors in MLP blocks that promote toxicity.\\nSimply subtracting these vectors from the residual stream can suppress toxic outputs.\\n\\nSecond, we applied DPO to our language model, using PPLM to carefully craft pairs of toxic and non-toxic continuations to Wikipedia prompts.\\n\\nThird, we studied how our aligned model\\\\dpo{}averts toxicity.\\nRather than removing the regions that elicit toxicity,\\\\dpo{}bypasses them by learning an\\\\emph{offset}.\\nSuch an offset is distributed amongst multiple value vectors, allowing minimal changes to every weight.\\nThis allows the model to preserve its pre-trained behavior, while averting toxic outputs, which matches the objective of the DPO loss.\\n\\nGiven this understanding, we demonstrated how to break the alignment of\\\\dpo{}, reverting it back to its toxic behavior.\\nNamely, we simply increase the regions that elicit toxicity, by scaling their corresponding key vectors.\\n\\nWe view our findings as a mechanistic case study for why aligned models can be jailbroken, and hope that this can lead to more robust alignment algorithms.\\nOur code, models, and data can be found at\\\\url{https://github.com/ajyl/dpo_toxic}.% Acknowledgements should only appear in the accepted version.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "9", "name": "Acknowledgements", 'encoding_format': 'float', 'input': ['{"id": "1", "name": "Introduction", "content": "\\\\label{sec:intro}Large language models learn surprising capabilities from pre-training on large datasets\\\\citep{gpt2FewShotLearners, chowdhery2023palm, touvron2023llama}.\\nWhile these capabilities lead to impressive achievements, they also include unwanted behaviors that can be found in large-scale web data, such as toxicity and bias\\\\citep{sheng-etal-2019-woman, gehman-etal-2020-realtoxicityprompts}.\\nAs a result, researchers have developed alignment algorithms to reduce undesirable behaviors, which often use reinforcement learning with human preferences (RLHF).\\nFor instance, proximal policy optimization (PPO,\\\\citealt{schulman2017proximal}) fits a reward model on human preference data, which is then used to fine-tune a language model, while direct preference optimization (DPO,\\\\citealt{rafailov2023direct}) by-passes the reward model and derives reward signals directly from pairwise preference data.\\n\\nWhile such algorithms can suppress undesirable behavior, our understanding of the mechanisms by which the undesirable behavior is suppressed is limited.\\nFurthermore, researchers have demonstrated that such alignments can be surprisingly easily undone\\\\citep{wallace-etal-2019-universal, zou2023universal, wei2023jailbroken, carlini2023are}.\\nWhile prior work hypothesize why jailbreaks are possible through empirical studies~\\\\citep{wei2023jailbroken}, in this work we provide a mechanistic explanation for such phenomena.\\n\\n\\nGiven the above limitations, in this work we study the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms alter a model\'s behavior.\\nResearchers have demonstrated that a deep enough understanding of a model\'s inner representations allows us to interpret how it makes decisions.\\nFor instance, various concepts such as world models, truthfulness, or even task-specific features have highly interpretable and controllable representations\\\\citep{li2023inference, todd2023function, nanda2023emergent}.\\nMotivated by such findings, we study how the representation space of language models change by comparing it before and after an alignment algorithm is applied.\\nOur work relates to that of\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}, which studies how the capabilities of a language model changes after fine-tuning on synthetic tasks.\\nUnlike this previous work, we study the change in mechanisms from a RLHF algorithm on a natural language setting.\\n\\nWe consider DPO and toxicity as a case-study of RLHF alignment algorithms.\\nWe first study how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2-medium (henceforth GPT2).\\nWe then apply DPO using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset that consists of toxic and nontoxic samples.\\nLastly, we study the mechanisms by which toxicity is no longer generated after DPO, and how those mechanisms can fail.\\n\\n\\nOur work is organized as follows: in Section~\\\\ref{sec:preliminaries}we provide the necessary preliminaries relevant to our ', 'of a language model changes after fine-tuning on synthetic tasks.\\nUnlike this previous work, we study the change in mechanisms from a RLHF algorithm on a natural language setting.\\n\\nWe consider DPO and toxicity as a case-study of RLHF alignment algorithms.\\nWe first study how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2-medium (henceforth GPT2).\\nWe then apply DPO using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset that consists of toxic and nontoxic samples.\\nLastly, we study the mechanisms by which toxicity is no longer generated after DPO, and how those mechanisms can fail.\\n\\n\\nOur work is organized as follows: in Section~\\\\ref{sec:preliminaries}we provide the necessary preliminaries relevant to our work.\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}, we demonstrate how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2.\\nWe find multiple vectors in multilayer perceptron (MLP) blocks that promote toxicity.\\nWe apply singular value decomposition (SVD) to these toxic vectors to find vectors that represent specific dimensions of toxicity in the model.\\nTo validate the role of these vectors in generating toxic outputs, we intervene with our toxic vectors and demonstrate much safer outputs.\\n\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:dpo}, we explain our procedure to apply DPO on our language models to reduce toxicity, using a carefully crafted pairwise toxicity dataset, produced by using PPLM~\\\\citep{dathathri2019plug}to generate paired toxic and non-toxic samples.\\n\\n\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:post_dpo}, we demonstrate how toxicity is no longer elicited after DPO.\\nNamely, we show that every parameter is minimally shifted, including the toxic vectors.\\nHowever, such minimal changes in weights allow the model to avert the triggering of toxic vectors.\\nPut differently, DPO\\\\emph{does not remove}the capability of generating toxic outputs, but learns an``offset\'\', distributed amongst its layers, to``bypass\'\'the regions that elicit toxicity.\\nBased on this understanding, we demonstrate the ease of re-activating these vectors to generate toxic outputs, and thus undoing the alignment learned from DPO.\\nWe view our findings as shedding light into why aligned models can be jailbroken or un-aligned.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "2", "name": "Preliminaries", "content": "\\\\label{sec:preliminaries}In this section we provide background and notations, much of which is borrowed from\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}.\\\\paragraph{Transformers, MLPs.}Transformer-based language models typically consists of embedding and unembedding layers$E, U \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{|\\\\mathcal{V}|\\\\times d}$with a series of$L$transformer layers in-between\\\\citep{attentionIsAllYouNeed}.\\nEach layer$l$consists of attention heads and a multilayer perception (MLP) layer.\\n\\nGiven an input sequence${\\\\mathbf{w} = \\\\langle w_0, ..., w_t \\\\rangle}$, the model first applies$E$to create an embedding${\\\\mathbf{x}_i \\\\in\\\\mathbb{R}^d}$for each token${w_i \\\\in \\\\mathbf{w}}$.\\nWe call${\\\\mathbf{x}_i}$the residual stream.\\n\\nThe residual stream is then updated by attention heads and MLP blocks from subsequent layers (bias terms omitted):$$\\n    \\\\mathbf{x^{\\\\ell+1}_i} = x^\\\\ell_i + \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(x^\\\\ell_i + \\\\texttt{Att}^\\\\ell(x^\\\\ell_i))\\n$$When needed, we specify the intermittent residual stream at layer$\\\\ell$(after the attention head, before the MLP) as$\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell\\\\_mid}$.\\nPer\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}, the updates to the residual stream from each MLP block can be further decomposed.\\nNamely, MLP blocks consist of two linear transformations, with point-wise activations$\\\\sigma$in-between:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp}\\n    \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(\\\\mathbf{x}^\\\\ell) = \\\\sigma\\\\left(W_K^\\\\ell\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\right) W_V^\\\\ell,\\n\\\\end{align}where$W_K^\\\\ell, W_V^\\\\ell \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d_{mlp} \\\\times d}$.\\nWe notate the$i$-th row in$W_K$as$\\\\text{MLP.}\\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}$and refer to them as key-vectors, and the$i$-th column in$W_V$,$\\\\text{MLP.}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}$, as value-vectors (we sometimes omit``$\\\\text{MLP}$\'\'and just use$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}, \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$).\\n\\nEquation~\\\\eqref{eq:mlp}indicates that\\\\emph{the output of MLP blocks is the sum of its value vectors $\\\\mathbf{v}_i$, each scaled by a coefficient value $m_i^\\\\ell$}, where$\\\\mathbf{m}^\\\\ell := \\\\sigma\\\\left(W_K^\\\\ell \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\right) \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d_{mlp}}$:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp_as_sum_of_value_vectors}\\n  \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}) = \\\\sum_{i=1}^{d_{mlp}} \\\\sigma(\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}) \\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} = \\\\sum_{i=1}^{d_{mlp}} m_i^{\\\\ell} \\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}.\\n\\\\end{align}Put differently, the MLP block writes to the residual stream$d_{mlp}$times, once for each value vector.\\nWe call each of these updates a\\\\emph{sub-update}.\\n\\nAll of our experiments are conducted with GPT2-medium, which has$L = 24$,$d = 1024$, and$d_{mlp} = 4096$.\\\\paragraph{Interpreting Value Vectors in Vocabulary Space.}\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that for each sub-update, each value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$either promotes or suppresses the likelihood of a token$w$from being generated:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:value_vecs_promote}\\n  p&\\\\big(w \\\\;|\\\\; \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}, E\\\\big) &\\\\propto \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)} \\\\cdot \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}\\n  \\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}where$\\\\mathbf{e}_w$is the embedding of$w$.\\nThis indicates that when${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} > 0}$, the likelihood of$w$increases, while${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} < 0}$decreases the likelihood.\\\\footnote{See Appendix for derivation.}Further note that this dot product can be further decomposed.\\nNamely,$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is a``static\'\'value that does not depend on the input: only when$\\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is scaled by$m_i$(which is determined by the its corresponding key vector,$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell$, and the residual stream$\\\\textbf{x}$) do we see the impact of the ', 'in Vocabulary Space.}\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that for each sub-update, each value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$either promotes or suppresses the likelihood of a token$w$from being generated:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:value_vecs_promote}\\n  p&\\\\big(w \\\\;|\\\\; \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}, E\\\\big) &\\\\propto \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)} \\\\cdot \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}\\n  \\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}where$\\\\mathbf{e}_w$is the embedding of$w$.\\nThis indicates that when${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} > 0}$, the likelihood of$w$increases, while${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} < 0}$decreases the likelihood.\\\\footnote{See Appendix for derivation.}Further note that this dot product can be further decomposed.\\nNamely,$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is a``static\'\'value that does not depend on the input: only when$\\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is scaled by$m_i$(which is determined by the its corresponding key vector,$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell$, and the residual stream$\\\\textbf{x}$) do we see the impact of the input towards the likelihood of$w$.\\n\\nThus the projection${\\\\mathbf{r}_i^\\\\ell = E \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{|\\\\mathcal{V}|}}$induces a ranking of tokens that get promoted by value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$, in which tokens with the highest dot products$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i$are promoted most by value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$.\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}we show value vectors that promote toxicity by applying these projections.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "3", "name": "Toxicity in Pre-trained Language Models", "content": "\\\\label{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}In this "Extracting Toxic Vectors", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:extracting_toxic_vectors}\\\\paragraph{Toxicity Probe Vector.}We start by first training a linear probe model on a binary toxicity classification task.\\nNamely, we use the Jigsaw toxic comment classification dataset\\\\citep{jigsaw-toxic-comment-classification-challenge}, which consists of 561,808 comments, each of which is labeled as toxic or non-toxic.\\nWe use a 90:10 split for training and validation.\\nWe train our probe model,\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}, on the residual stream in the last layer, averaged across all timesteps ($\\\\mathbf{\\\\bar{x}}^{L-1}$):\\\\begin{align}\\nP(\\\\text{Toxic} | \\\\mathbf{\\\\bar{x}}^{L-1}) = \\\\text{softmax}(W_{\\\\text{Toxic}}\\\\mathbf{\\\\bar{x}}^{L-1}), W_{\\\\text{Toxic}} \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d}\\n\\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}Our probe vector achieves an accuracy of 94\\\\%on the validation split.\\nWe view our toxic probe vector\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}as an aggregate of all the relevant signals in the language model to classify an input as toxic.\\\\paragraph{Toxic Vectors in MLP Blocks.}Given our probe vector\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}, we can use it to find weights within the language model that promote toxicity.\\nNamely,\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that value vectors promote tokens at a concept-level. \\nGiven this, we search for value vectors that promote toxicity, by checking for all value vectors with the highest cosine similarity with\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}.\\nWe find that Wikipedia sentences as prompts, we measure the harmonic mean between precision and recall of our model's output, where precision is the fraction of generated tokens contained in the original Wikipedia continuation, and recall is the fraction of tokens in the Wikipedia continuation contained in the model's generation.\\n\\nWith perplexity and F1, we hope to see minimal changes after our "content": "\\\\label{subsec:dpo_background}DPO relies on pairwise preference data, in which given a prompt, we have a preferred (positive) continuation and a non-preferred (negative) continuation.\\nGiven each preference pair, the algorithm promotes the likelihood of the positive sample, while suppressing the likelihood of the negative sample, using the following loss term:\\\\begin{equation}\\n\\\\begin{aligned}\\n    &\\\\mathcal{L}_\\\\text{DPO} = -\\\\mathbb{E}\\\\left[\\\\log \\\\sigma \\\\left(\\\\beta \\\\log P - \\\\beta \\\\log N\\\\right)\\\\right] \\\\nonumber, \\\\\\\\\\n    &P = \\\\frac{\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}(y_+\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}{\\\\pi_{ref}(y_+\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}, N = \\\\frac{\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}(y_-\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}{\\\\pi_{ref}(y_-\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})},\\n\\\\end{aligned}\\n\\\\end{equation}where$y_+$and$y_-$are preferred (nontoxic) and non-preferred (toxic) continuations of$\\\\mathbf{w}$,$\\\\pi_{ref}$is the frozen weights of the original language model, and$\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}$is the weights of the language model being updated (See\\\\citet{rafailov2023direct}for details).\\nThe algorithm promotes the likelihood of$P$, while suppressing the likelihood of$N$.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "4.2", "name": "Constructing Pairwise Toxic Data", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:construct_dpo_data}We build our pairwise toxicity dataset using PPLM\\\\citep{dathathri2019plug}.\\nPPLM is an attribute-controlled language generation technique, which attaches a simple linear attribute classification layer,$p(a|\\\\mathbf{w})$onto a language model to guide its generation.\\nDuring generation, PPLM uses the attribute classifier to compute the gradients that increases the likelihood of the language model\'s output to contain the desired attribute$a$, and shifts the activations in such direction (See\\\\citet{dathathri2019plug}for details):\\\\useshortskip$$p(y\\\\mid a) \\\\propto p(y)p(a \\\\mid y)$$To generate pairwise preference data, we use sentences from Wikitext-2\\\\citep{merity2016pointer}as prompts.\\nFor each prompt, we generate a positive sample using greedy sampling with GPT2, while using PPLM to generate negative (toxic) samples.\\nWe use our toxic probe\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}as our attribute classifier to guide towards toxic outputs.\\nWe create 24,576 pairs of toxic and nontoxic continuations.\\\\footnote{We release this data to enable further studies.}We train until validation loss converges with a patience value of 10, which occurs after approximately 6,000 sample pairs.\\nAppendix~\\\\ref{sec:hyperparams}has details for DPO and PPLM hyperparameters.\\\\begin{figure}\\n\\\\vskip "\\\\label{subsec:toxic_vectors_after_dpo}Of the toxic vectors described in Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}, note that\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}are actual weights of the model. \\nThus we inspect how these vectors change after DPO.%We notate the toxic MLP vectors in the original language model as \\\\mlpgpt{} and those from the DPO model as \\\\mlpdpo{}.Interestingly, we find that every parameter in GPT2 and\\\\dpo{}has barely changed, including token embeddings, MLP blocks, and attention heads.\\nEvery parameter in GPT2 and its counterpart in\\\\dpo{}has a cosine similarity score greater than 0.99 and on average a norm difference less than 1e-5.\\\\footnote{The unembedding layer is the only exception, where the norm difference is less than 1e-3.}This applies for$\\\\text{MLP}.\\\\mathbf{k}_{\\\\text{Toxic}}$and\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}as well--toxic MLP vectors\\\\textbf{do not change}from DPO.\\n\\nPut differently, although toxicity is reduced by DPO, the ability to elicit toxicity with these value vectors still remain.\\nSo how is it that\\\\dpo{}averts toxic outputs?\\nThough its parameters have barely moved, below we show that their collective movement is enough to avoid toxic outputs.\\\\begin{figure}\\n\\\\vskip Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_jailbreak}We view our work as providing a mechanistic explanation for why aligned models can be undone or jailbroken--in our experiments, the regions that previously elicited toxic behavior does not change after DPO.\\nRather,\\\\dpo{}learns minimal changes spread across layers to avoid such regions and receive its reward.\\n\\nWith such knowledge, we conjecture that more robust alignment algorithms can be designed.\\nFor instance, can we eliminate undesirable regions, as opposed to bypassing them?\\nIn scenarios like ours, in which we can identify the weights that elicit undesirable outputs, what happens if we only updated those weights in isolation?\\nSimilarly, if DPO merely learned an offset that avoids toxic regions, can we replicate this behavior by only updating the bias terms?\\n\\nAlternatively, prior to deploying language models, perhaps we can add``suppression heads\'\'--layers that suppress undesirable behavior.\\nWhat would happen if we only updated late layers (or added layers) during alignment?\\n\\nLastly, can we characterize``jailbreak-ability\'\'or``unalign-ability\'\'of aligned models, without relying on test samples?\\n\\nWe leave these questions for future work.%\\\\subsection{Characterizing Robustness}%\\\\label{subsec:measuring_alignment_robustness}%If alignment robustness is the goal, how should we characterize robustness?%The answer may depend on the attack.%To the best of our knowledge, current literature lacks a taxonomy of adversarial cases.%For instance, whether an adversary only has access to an API, or to the weights of a model can lead to different attacks.%Our safety designs may depend largely on such characteristics.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "6.2", "name": "On the Role of KL-Divergence Regularization", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_kl_div}We hypothesize that the minimal changes distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not ', 'distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not entail a KL-divergence term.\\nPerhaps this allows the model to make drastic and localized changes, such as in late layers, as opposed to distributed, minimal changes.%\\\\subsection{On Generalizing Findings from Our Case Study}%\\\\label{subsec:generalizing_findings_from_case_study}%We note that our findings are from a case study, on a specific language model, task, and data set.%An obvious extension would be to study a larger model.%While in principle, we do not expect the "subsection": [{"id": "7.1", "name": "Alignment Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_alignment_algorithms}Numerous alignment algorithms have been proposed, and the choice of algorithm may largely depend on the type of data available.\\nPerhaps most commonly, human feedback data is used\\\\citep{stiennon2020learning, ouyang2022training, touvron2023llama}for methods such as PPO\\\\citep{schulman2017proximal}or DPO\\\\citep{rafailov2023direct}.\\nWhen labels for only undesirable behavior is available, algorithms like unlikelihood training\\\\citep{Welleck2020Neural}or Cringe\\\\citep{adolphs-etal-2023-cringe, xu2023some}can be used.\\nWe study DPO because it is easy to use and currently widely used.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.2", "name": "Mechanistic Interpretability", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_mech_interp}The goal of mechanistic interpretability is largely to reverse engineer model behaviors\\\\citep{olah2020zoom, elhage2021mathematical, geva-etal-2021-transformer}.\\nBy doing so, researchers have uncovered various interpretable and controllable representations, such as world models\\\\citep{li2023emergent, nanda2023emergent},``truthfulness\'\'\\\\citep{li2023inference}, knowledge\\\\citep{meng2022locating, hernandez2023linearity, burns2023discovering, geva2023dissecting}, linguistic properties\\\\citep{conneau-etal-2018-cram, tenney-etal-2019-bert}, or even tasks\\\\citep{ilharco2022editing, hendel-etal-2023-context, todd2023function}.\\n\\nRather than probing for specific representations, researchers have also characterized the representations of language models from a geometric perspective\\\\citep{park2023linear}.\\\\citet{balestriero2023characterizing}demonstrate a geometric characterization that can be used to extract feature representations that solve toxicity detection.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}study the mechanisms in which fine-tuning on synthetic tasks alters the model\'s capabilities.\\nWe study the effects of RLHF on a more realistic, natural language setting.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.3", "name": "Jailbreaking Aligned Models", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_jailbreak}Researchers demonstrated that aligned models can be surprisingly easily jailbroken\\\\citep{wallace-etal-2019-universal, zou2023universal, wei2023jailbroken, carlini2023are}.\\nSuch adversarial attacks typically involve searching for prompts that can elicit previously unlearned behaviors, or even personal information\\\\citep{nasr2023scalable}.\\\\citet{carlini2023are}show that multimodal models can also be jailbroken.\\\\citet{wei2023jailbroken}provide hypotheses, backed by empirical studies, as to why language models can be jailbroken.\\n\\nIn a similar vein to jailbreaks, numerous researchers have demonstrated that aligned models can easily be un-aligned\\\\citep{yang2023shadow, qi2023fine}, sometimes with as few as 100 fine-tuning examples.\\nWe view our work as adding a mechanistic understanding of such phenomena.", "subsection": []}]}', '{"id": "8", "name": "Conclusion", "content": "\\\\label{sec:conclusion}In this work we studied the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms unlearn a capability, taking DPO and toxicity as a case study.\\nFirst, we uncovered how toxicity is represented and elicited in a pre-trained language model.\\nWe find numerous vectors in MLP blocks that promote toxicity.\\nSimply subtracting these vectors from the residual stream can suppress toxic outputs.\\n\\nSecond, we applied DPO to our language model, using PPLM to carefully craft pairs of toxic and non-toxic continuations to Wikipedia prompts.\\n\\nThird, we studied how our aligned model\\\\dpo{}averts toxicity.\\nRather than removing the regions that elicit toxicity,\\\\dpo{}bypasses them by learning an\\\\emph{offset}.\\nSuch an offset is distributed amongst multiple value vectors, allowing minimal changes to every weight.\\nThis allows the model to preserve its pre-trained behavior, while averting toxic outputs, which matches the objective of the DPO loss.\\n\\nGiven this understanding, we demonstrated how to break the alignment of\\\\dpo{}, reverting it back to its toxic behavior.\\nNamely, we simply increase the regions that elicit toxicity, by scaling their corresponding key vectors.\\n\\nWe view our findings as a mechanistic case study for why aligned models can be jailbroken, and hope that this can lead to more robust alignment algorithms.\\nOur code, models, and data can be found at\\\\url{https://github.com/ajyl/dpo_toxic}.% Acknowledgements should only appear in the accepted version.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "9", "name": "Acknowledgements", "content": "We thank Ekdeep Singh Lubana for fruitful discussions, and Santiago Serra Castro for helping with figures.\\nThis work was supported via NSF under grant\\\\#2306372.%\\\\textbf{Do not} learn surprising capabilities from pre-training on large datasets\\\\citep{gpt2FewShotLearners, chowdhery2023palm, touvron2023llama}.\\nWhile these capabilities lead to impressive achievements, they also include unwanted behaviors that can be found in large-scale web data, such as toxicity and bias\\\\citep{sheng-etal-2019-woman, gehman-etal-2020-realtoxicityprompts}.\\nAs a result, researchers have developed alignment algorithms to reduce undesirable behaviors, which often use reinforcement learning with human preferences (RLHF).\\nFor instance, proximal policy optimization (PPO,\\\\citealt{schulman2017proximal}) fits a reward model on human preference data, which is then used to fine-tune a language model, while direct preference optimization (DPO,\\\\citealt{rafailov2023direct}) by-passes the reward model and derives reward signals directly from pairwise preference data.\\n\\nWhile such algorithms can suppress undesirable behavior, our understanding of the mechanisms by which the undesirable behavior is suppressed is limited.\\nFurthermore, researchers have demonstrated that such alignments can be surprisingly easily undone\\\\citep{wallace-etal-2019-universal, zou2023universal, wei2023jailbroken, carlini2023are}.\\nWhile prior work hypothesize why jailbreaks are possible through empirical studies~\\\\citep{wei2023jailbroken}, in this work we provide a mechanistic explanation for such phenomena.\\n\\n\\nGiven the above limitations, in this work we study the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms alter a model\'s behavior.\\nResearchers have demonstrated that a deep enough understanding of a model\'s inner representations allows us to interpret how it makes decisions.\\nFor instance, various concepts such as world models, truthfulness, or even task-specific features have highly interpretable and controllable representations\\\\citep{li2023inference, todd2023function, nanda2023emergent}.\\nMotivated by such findings, we study how the representation space of language models change by comparing it before and after an alignment algorithm is applied.\\nOur work relates to that of\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}, which studies how the capabilities of a language model changes after fine-tuning on synthetic tasks.\\nUnlike this previous work, we study the change in mechanisms from a RLHF algorithm on a natural language setting.\\n\\nWe consider DPO and toxicity as a case-study of RLHF alignment algorithms.\\nWe first study how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2-medium (henceforth GPT2).\\nWe then apply DPO using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset that consists of toxic and nontoxic samples.\\nLastly, we study the mechanisms by which toxicity is no longer generated after DPO, and how those mechanisms can fail.\\n\\n\\nOur work is organized as follows: in Section~\\\\ref{sec:preliminaries}we provide the necessary preliminaries setting.\\n\\nWe consider DPO and toxicity as a case-study of RLHF alignment algorithms.\\nWe first study how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2-medium (henceforth GPT2).\\nWe then apply DPO using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset that consists of toxic and nontoxic samples.\\nLastly, we study the mechanisms by which toxicity is no longer generated after DPO, and how those mechanisms can fail.\\n\\n\\nOur work is organized as follows: in Section~\\\\ref{sec:preliminaries}we provide the necessary preliminaries relevant to our work.\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}, we demonstrate how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2.\\nWe find multiple vectors in multilayer perceptron (MLP) blocks that promote toxicity.\\nWe apply singular value decomposition (SVD) to these toxic vectors to find vectors that represent specific dimensions of toxicity in the model.\\nTo validate the role of these vectors in reduce toxicity, using a carefully crafted pairwise toxicity dataset, produced by using PPLM~\\\\citep{dathathri2019plug}to generate paired toxic and non-toxic samples.\\n\\n\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:post_dpo}, we demonstrate how toxicity is no longer elicited after DPO.\\nNamely, we show that every parameter is minimally shifted, including the toxic vectors.\\nHowever, such minimal changes in weights allow the model to avert the triggering of toxic vectors.\\nPut differently, DPO\\\\emph{does not remove}the capability of generating toxic outputs, but learns an``offset\'\', distributed amongst its layers, to``bypass\'\'the regions that elicit toxicity.\\nBased on this understanding, we demonstrate the ease of re-activating these vectors to generate toxic "Preliminaries", "content": "\\\\label{sec:preliminaries}In this section we provide background and notations, much of which is borrowed from\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}.\\\\paragraph{Transformers, MLPs.}Transformer-based language models typically consists of embedding and unembedding layers$E, U \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{|\\\\mathcal{V}|\\\\times d}$with a series of$L$transformer layers in-between\\\\citep{attentionIsAllYouNeed}.\\nEach layer$l$consists of attention heads and a multilayer perception (MLP) layer.\\n\\nGiven an input sequence${\\\\mathbf{w} = \\\\langle w_0, ..., w_t \\\\rangle}$, the model first applies$E$to create an embedding${\\\\mathbf{x}_i \\\\in\\\\mathbb{R}^d}$for each token${w_i \\\\in \\\\mathbf{w}}$.\\nWe call${\\\\mathbf{x}_i}$the residual stream.\\n\\nThe residual stream is then updated by attention heads and MLP blocks from subsequent layers (bias terms omitted):$$\\n    \\\\mathbf{x^{\\\\ell+1}_i} = x^\\\\ell_i + \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(x^\\\\ell_i + \\\\texttt{Att}^\\\\ell(x^\\\\ell_i))\\n$$When needed, we specify the intermittent residual stream at layer$\\\\ell$(after the attention head, before the MLP) as$\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell\\\\_mid}$.\\nPer\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}, the updates to the residual stream from each MLP block can be further decomposed.\\nNamely, MLP blocks consist of two linear transformations, with point-wise activations$\\\\sigma$in-between:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp}\\n    \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(\\\\mathbf{x}^\\\\ell) = \\\\sigma\\\\left(W_K^\\\\ell\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\right) W_V^\\\\ell,\\n\\\\end{align}where$W_K^\\\\ell, W_V^\\\\ell \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d_{mlp} \\\\times d}$.\\nWe notate the$i$-th row in$W_K$as$\\\\text{MLP.}\\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}$and refer to them as key-vectors, and the$i$-th column in$W_V$,$\\\\text{MLP.}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}$, as value-vectors (we sometimes omit``$\\\\text{MLP}$\'\'and just use$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}, \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$).\\n\\nEquation~\\\\eqref{eq:mlp}indicates that\\\\emph{the output of MLP blocks is the sum of its value vectors $\\\\mathbf{v}_i$, each scaled by a coefficient value $m_i^\\\\ell$}, where$\\\\mathbf{m}^\\\\ell := \\\\sigma\\\\left(W_K^\\\\ell \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\right) \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d_{mlp}}$:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp_as_sum_of_value_vectors}\\n  \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}) = \\\\sum_{i=1}^{d_{mlp}} \\\\sigma(\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}) \\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} = \\\\sum_{i=1}^{d_{mlp}} m_i^{\\\\ell} \\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}.\\n\\\\end{align}Put differently, the MLP block writes to the residual stream$d_{mlp}$times, once for each value vector.\\nWe call each of these updates a\\\\emph{sub-update}.\\n\\nAll of our experiments are conducted with GPT2-medium, which has$L = 24$,$d = 1024$, and$d_{mlp} = 4096$.\\\\paragraph{Interpreting Value Vectors in Vocabulary Space.}\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that for each sub-update, each value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$either promotes or suppresses the likelihood of a token$w$from being generated:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:value_vecs_promote}\\n  p&\\\\big(w \\\\;|\\\\; \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}, E\\\\big) &\\\\propto \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)} \\\\cdot \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}\\n  \\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}where$\\\\mathbf{e}_w$is the embedding of$w$.\\nThis indicates that when${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} > 0}$, the likelihood of$w$increases, while${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} < 0}$decreases the likelihood.\\\\footnote{See Appendix for derivation.}Further note that this dot product can be further decomposed.\\nNamely,$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is a``static\'\'value that does not depend on the input: only when$\\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is scaled by$m_i$(which is determined by the its corresponding key vector,$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell$, and the residual stream$\\\\textbf{x}$) do we see the impact of the ', 'in Vocabulary Space.}\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that for each sub-update, each value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$either promotes or suppresses the likelihood of a token$w$from being generated:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:value_vecs_promote}\\n  p&\\\\big(w \\\\;|\\\\; \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}, E\\\\big) &\\\\propto \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)} \\\\cdot \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}\\n  \\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}where$\\\\mathbf{e}_w$is the embedding of$w$.\\nThis indicates that when${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} > 0}$, the likelihood of$w$increases, while${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} < 0}$decreases the likelihood.\\\\footnote{See Appendix for derivation.}Further note that this dot product can be further decomposed.\\nNamely,$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is a``static\'\'value that does not depend on the input: only when$\\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is scaled by$m_i$(which is determined by the its corresponding key vector,$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell$, and the residual stream$\\\\textbf{x}$) do we see the impact of the input towards the likelihood of$w$.\\n\\nThus the projection${\\\\mathbf{r}_i^\\\\ell = E \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{|\\\\mathcal{V}|}}$induces a ranking of tokens that get promoted by value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$, in which tokens with the highest dot products$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i$are promoted most by value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$.\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}we show value vectors that promote toxicity by applying these projections.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "3", "name": "Toxicity in Pre-trained Language Models", "content": "\\\\label{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}In this section we demonstrate how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2, by introducing a series of vectors that can be extracted from the language model.", "subsection": [{"id": "3.1", "name": "Extracting Toxic Vectors", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:extracting_toxic_vectors}\\\\paragraph{Toxicity Probe Vector.}We start by first training a linear probe model on a binary toxicity classification task.\\nNamely, we use the Jigsaw toxic comment classification dataset\\\\citep{jigsaw-toxic-comment-classification-challenge}, which consists of 561,808 comments, each of which is labeled as toxic or non-toxic.\\nWe use a 90:10 split for training and validation.\\nWe train our probe model,\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}, on the residual stream in the last layer, averaged across all timesteps ($\\\\mathbf{\\\\bar{x}}^{L-1}$):\\\\begin{align}\\nP(\\\\text{Toxic} | \\\\mathbf{\\\\bar{x}}^{L-1}) = \\\\text{softmax}(W_{\\\\text{Toxic}}\\\\mathbf{\\\\bar{x}}^{L-1}), W_{\\\\text{Toxic}} \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d}\\n\\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}Our probe vector achieves an accuracy of 94\\\\%on the validation split.\\nWe view our toxic probe vector\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}as an aggregate of all the relevant signals in the language model to classify an input as toxic.\\\\paragraph{Toxic Vectors in MLP Blocks.}Given our probe vector\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}, we can use it to find weights within the language model that promote toxicity.\\nNamely,\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that value vectors promote tokens at a concept-level. \\nGiven this, we search for value vectors that promote toxicity, by checking for all value vectors with the highest cosine similarity with\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}.\\nWe find that indeed, there are value vectors that promote toxic tokens (See Section~\\\\ref{subsec:toxic_vecs_in_vocab_space}).\\nWe notate our set of toxic value vectors as\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}and their corresponding key vectors as\\\\toxicmlpkeysymbol{}.\\n\\nWe provide two perspectives of our\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}vectors: 1) when triggered, they promote the likelihood of toxic tokens to be generated, and 2)\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}are vectors within the model that contribute towards the\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}direction.\\\\paragraph{SVD: Decomposed Toxic Vectors.}After extracting a set of N (=128)\\\\footnote{We experiment with different values for N, and get similar results.}\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}vectors, we stack them into a$N \\\\times d$matrix.\\nWe then apply singular value decomposition to get decomposed singular value vectors\\\\toxicsvdsymbol{}.\\nWe refer to the$i$-th singular value vector as\\\\toxicsvdsymbol{}[$i$].\\nWe view\\\\toxicsvdsymbol{}as basis vectors that span the toxicity representation space within the language model.\\\\begin{table}[t]\\n\\\\caption{Top toxic vectors projected onto the vocabulary space. \\\\textcolor{red}{WARNING: THESE "content": "\\\\label{subsec:dpo_background}DPO relies on pairwise preference data, in which given a prompt, we have a preferred (positive) continuation and a non-preferred (negative) continuation.\\nGiven each preference pair, the algorithm promotes the likelihood of the positive sample, while suppressing the likelihood of the negative sample, using the following loss term:\\\\begin{equation}\\n\\\\begin{aligned}\\n    &\\\\mathcal{L}_\\\\text{DPO} = -\\\\mathbb{E}\\\\left[\\\\log \\\\sigma \\\\left(\\\\beta \\\\log P - \\\\beta \\\\log N\\\\right)\\\\right] \\\\nonumber, \\\\\\\\\\n    &P = \\\\frac{\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}(y_+\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}{\\\\pi_{ref}(y_+\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}, N = \\\\frac{\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}(y_-\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}{\\\\pi_{ref}(y_-\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})},\\n\\\\end{aligned}\\n\\\\end{equation}where$y_+$and$y_-$are preferred (nontoxic) and non-preferred (toxic) continuations of$\\\\mathbf{w}$,$\\\\pi_{ref}$is the frozen weights of the original language model, and$\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}$is the weights of the language model being updated (See\\\\citet{rafailov2023direct}for details).\\nThe algorithm promotes the likelihood of$P$, while suppressing the likelihood of$N$.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "4.2", "name": "Constructing Pairwise Toxic Data", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:construct_dpo_data}We build our pairwise toxicity dataset using PPLM\\\\citep{dathathri2019plug}.\\nPPLM is an attribute-controlled language generation technique, which attaches a simple linear attribute classification layer,$p(a|\\\\mathbf{w})$onto a language model to guide its generation.\\nDuring generation, PPLM uses the attribute classifier to compute the gradients that increases the likelihood of the language model\'s output to contain the desired attribute$a$, and shifts the activations in such direction (See\\\\citet{dathathri2019plug}for details):\\\\useshortskip$$p(y\\\\mid a) \\\\propto p(y)p(a \\\\mid y)$$To generate pairwise preference data, we use sentences from Wikitext-2\\\\citep{merity2016pointer}as prompts.\\nFor each prompt, we generate a positive sample using greedy sampling with GPT2, while using PPLM to generate negative (toxic) samples.\\nWe use our toxic probe\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}as our attribute classifier to guide towards toxic outputs.\\nWe create 24,576 pairs of toxic and nontoxic continuations.\\\\footnote{We release this data to enable further studies.}We train until validation loss converges with a patience value of 10, which occurs after approximately 6,000 sample pairs.\\nAppendix~\\\\ref{sec:hyperparams}has details for DPO and PPLM hyperparameters.\\\\begin{figure}\\n\\\\vskip 0.2in\\n\\\\begin{center}\\n\\\\centerline{\\\\includegraphics[width=\\\\columnwidth]{figures/logitlens.pdf}}\\n\\\\caption{Logit lens on GPT2 and \\\\dpo{}. Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_jailbreak}We view our work as providing a mechanistic explanation for why aligned models can be undone or jailbroken--in our experiments, the regions that previously elicited toxic behavior does not change after DPO.\\nRather,\\\\dpo{}learns minimal changes spread across layers to avoid such regions and receive its reward.\\n\\nWith such knowledge, we conjecture that more robust alignment algorithms can be designed.\\nFor instance, can we eliminate undesirable regions, as opposed to bypassing them?\\nIn scenarios like ours, in which we can identify the weights that elicit undesirable outputs, what happens if we only updated those weights in isolation?\\nSimilarly, if DPO merely learned an offset that avoids toxic regions, can we replicate this behavior by only updating the bias terms?\\n\\nAlternatively, prior to deploying language models, perhaps we can add``suppression heads\'\'--layers that suppress undesirable behavior.\\nWhat would happen if we only updated late layers (or added layers) during alignment?\\n\\nLastly, can we characterize``jailbreak-ability\'\'or``unalign-ability\'\'of aligned models, without relying on test samples?\\n\\nWe leave these questions for future work.%\\\\subsection{Characterizing Robustness}%\\\\label{subsec:measuring_alignment_robustness}%If alignment robustness is the goal, how should we characterize robustness?%The answer may depend on the attack.%To the best of our knowledge, current literature lacks a taxonomy of adversarial cases.%For instance, whether an adversary only has access to an API, or to the weights of a model can lead to different attacks.%Our safety designs may depend largely on such characteristics.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "6.2", "name": "On the Role of KL-Divergence Regularization", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_kl_div}We hypothesize that the minimal changes distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not ', 'distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not entail a KL-divergence term.\\nPerhaps this allows the model to make drastic and localized changes, such as in late layers, as opposed to distributed, minimal changes.%\\\\subsection{On Generalizing Findings from Our Case Study}%\\\\label{subsec:generalizing_findings_from_case_study}%We note that our findings are from a case study, on a specific language model, task, and data set.%An obvious extension would be to study a larger model.%While in principle, we do not expect the mechanisms of DPO to differ for a larger model, we have also seen that the scale of models lead to surprising results \\\\citep{hoffmann2022training, wei2022emergent}.%We leave this for future work.", "subsection": []}]}', '{"id": "7", "name": "Related Work", "content": "\\\\label{sec:related_work}%JKK: Better to leave this out and get straight into it%In this section we discuss related work.", "subsection": [{"id": "7.1", "name": "Alignment Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_alignment_algorithms}Numerous alignment algorithms have been proposed, and the choice of algorithm may largely depend on the type of data available.\\nPerhaps most commonly, human feedback data is used\\\\citep{stiennon2020learning, ouyang2022training, touvron2023llama}for methods such as PPO\\\\citep{schulman2017proximal}or DPO\\\\citep{rafailov2023direct}.\\nWhen labels for only undesirable behavior is available, algorithms like unlikelihood training\\\\citep{Welleck2020Neural}or Cringe\\\\citep{adolphs-etal-2023-cringe, xu2023some}can be used.\\nWe study DPO because it is easy to use and currently widely used.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.2", "name": "Mechanistic Interpretability", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_mech_interp}The goal of mechanistic interpretability is largely to reverse engineer model behaviors\\\\citep{olah2020zoom, elhage2021mathematical, geva-etal-2021-transformer}.\\nBy doing so, researchers have uncovered various interpretable and controllable representations, such as world models\\\\citep{li2023emergent, nanda2023emergent},``truthfulness\'\'\\\\citep{li2023inference}, knowledge\\\\citep{meng2022locating, hernandez2023linearity, burns2023discovering, geva2023dissecting}, linguistic properties\\\\citep{conneau-etal-2018-cram, tenney-etal-2019-bert}, or even tasks\\\\citep{ilharco2022editing, hendel-etal-2023-context, todd2023function}.\\n\\nRather than probing for specific representations, researchers have also characterized the representations of language models from a geometric perspective\\\\citep{park2023linear}.\\\\citet{balestriero2023characterizing}demonstrate a geometric characterization that can be used to extract feature representations that solve toxicity detection.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}study the mechanisms in which fine-tuning on synthetic tasks alters the model\'s capabilities.\\nWe study the effects of RLHF on a more realistic, natural language setting.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.3", "name": "Jailbreaking Aligned Models", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_jailbreak}Researchers demonstrated that aligned models can be surprisingly easily jailbroken\\\\citep{wallace-etal-2019-universal, zou2023universal, wei2023jailbroken, carlini2023are}.\\nSuch adversarial attacks typically involve searching for prompts that can elicit previously unlearned behaviors, or even personal information\\\\citep{nasr2023scalable}.\\\\citet{carlini2023are}show that multimodal models can also be jailbroken.\\\\citet{wei2023jailbroken}provide hypotheses, backed by empirical studies, as to why language models can be jailbroken.\\n\\nIn a similar vein to jailbreaks, numerous researchers have demonstrated that aligned models can easily be un-aligned\\\\citep{yang2023shadow, qi2023fine}, sometimes with as few as 100 fine-tuning examples.\\nWe view our work as adding a mechanistic understanding of such phenomena.", "subsection": []}]}', '{"id": "8", "name": "Conclusion", "content": "\\\\label{sec:conclusion}In this work we studied the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms unlearn a capability, taking DPO and toxicity as a case study.\\nFirst, we uncovered how toxicity is represented and elicited in a pre-trained language model.\\nWe find numerous vectors in MLP blocks that promote toxicity.\\nSimply subtracting these vectors from the residual stream can suppress toxic outputs.\\n\\nSecond, we applied DPO to our language model, using PPLM to carefully craft pairs of toxic and non-toxic continuations to Wikipedia prompts.\\n\\nThird, we studied how our aligned model\\\\dpo{}averts toxicity.\\nRather than removing the regions that elicit toxicity,\\\\dpo{}bypasses them by learning an\\\\emph{offset}.\\nSuch an offset is distributed amongst multiple value vectors, allowing minimal changes to every weight.\\nThis allows the model to preserve its pre-trained behavior, while averting toxic outputs, which matches the objective of the DPO loss.\\n\\nGiven this understanding, we demonstrated how to break the alignment of\\\\dpo{}, reverting it back to its toxic behavior.\\nNamely, we simply increase the regions that elicit toxicity, by scaling their corresponding key vectors.\\n\\nWe view our findings as a mechanistic case study for why aligned models can be jailbroken, and hope that this can lead to more robust alignment algorithms.\\nOur code, models, and data can be found at\\\\url{https://github.com/ajyl/dpo_toxic}.% Acknowledgements should only appear in the accepted version.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "9", "name": "Acknowledgements",
