Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_jailbreak}We view our work as providing a mechanistic explanation for why aligned models can be undone or jailbroken--in our experiments, the regions that previously elicited toxic behavior does not change after DPO.\\nRather,\\\\dpo{}learns minimal changes spread across layers to avoid such regions and receive its reward.\\n\\nWith such knowledge, we conjecture that more robust alignment algorithms can be designed.\\nFor instance, can we eliminate undesirable regions, as opposed to bypassing them?\\nIn scenarios like ours, in which we can identify the weights that elicit undesirable outputs, what happens if we only updated those weights in isolation?\\nSimilarly, if DPO merely learned an offset that avoids toxic regions, can we replicate this behavior by only updating the bias terms?\\n\\nAlternatively, prior to deploying language models, perhaps we can add``suppression heads\'\'--layers that suppress undesirable behavior.\\nWhat would happen if we only updated late layers (or added layers) during alignment?\\n\\nLastly, can we characterize``jailbreak-ability\'\'or``unalign-ability\'\'of aligned models, without relying on test samples?\\n\\nWe leave these questions for future work.%\\\\subsection{Characterizing Robustness}%\\\\label{subsec:measuring_alignment_robustness}%If alignment robustness is the goal, how should we characterize robustness?%The answer may depend on the attack.%To the best of our knowledge, current literature lacks a taxonomy of adversarial cases.%For instance, whether an adversary only has access to an API, or to the weights of a model can lead to different attacks.%Our safety designs may depend largely on such characteristics.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "6.2", "name": "On the Role of KL-Divergence Regularization", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_kl_div}We hypothesize that the minimal changes distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not ', 'distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not entail a KL-divergence term.\\nPerhaps this allows the model to make drastic and localized changes, such as in late layers, as opposed to distributed, minimal changes.%\\\\subsection{On Generalizing Findings from Our Case Study}%\\\\label{subsec:generalizing_findings_from_case_study}%We note that our findings are from a case study, on a specific language model, task, and data set.%An obvious extension would be to study a larger model.%While in principle, we do not expect the mechanisms of DPO to differ for a larger model, we have also seen that the scale of models lead to surprising results \\\\citep{hoffmann2022training, wei2022emergent}.%We leave this for future work.", "subsection": [{"id": "7.1", "name": "Alignment Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_alignment_algorithms}Numerous alignment algorithms have been proposed, and the choice of algorithm may largely depend on the type of data available.\\nPerhaps most commonly, human feedback data is used\\\\citep{stiennon2020learning, ouyang2022training, touvron2023llama}for methods such as PPO\\\\citep{schulman2017proximal}or DPO\\\\citep{rafailov2023direct}.\\nWhen labels for only undesirable behavior is available, algorithms like unlikelihood training\\\\citep{Welleck2020Neural}or Cringe\\\\citep{adolphs-etal-2023-cringe, xu2023some}can be used.\\nWe study DPO because it is easy to use and currently widely used.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.2", "name": "Mechanistic Interpretability", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_mech_interp}The goal of mechanistic interpretability is largely to reverse engineer model behaviors\\\\citep{olah2020zoom, elhage2021mathematical, geva-etal-2021-transformer}.\\nBy doing so, researchers have uncovered various interpretable and controllable representations, such as world models\\\\citep{li2023emergent, nanda2023emergent},``truthfulness\'\'\\\\citep{li2023inference}, knowledge\\\\citep{meng2022locating, hernandez2023linearity, burns2023discovering, geva2023dissecting}, linguistic properties\\\\citep{conneau-etal-2018-cram, tenney-etal-2019-bert}, or even tasks\\\\citep{ilharco2022editing, hendel-etal-2023-context, todd2023function}.\\n\\nRather than probing for specific representations, researchers have also characterized the representations of language models from a geometric perspective\\\\citep{park2023linear}.\\\\citet{balestriero2023characterizing}demonstrate a geometric characterization that can be used to extract feature representations that solve toxicity detection.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}study the mechanisms in which fine-tuning on synthetic tasks alters the model\'s capabilities.\\nWe study the effects of RLHF on a more realistic, natural language setting.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.3", "name": "Jailbreaking Aligned Models", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_jailbreak}Researchers demonstrated that aligned models can be surprisingly easily jailbroken\\\\citep{wallace-etal-2019-universal, zou2023universal, wei2023jailbroken, carlini2023are}.\\nSuch adversarial attacks typically involve searching for prompts that can elicit previously unlearned behaviors, or even personal information\\\\citep{nasr2023scalable}.\\\\citet{carlini2023are}show that multimodal models can also be jailbroken.\\\\citet{wei2023jailbroken}provide hypotheses, backed by empirical studies, as to why language models can be jailbroken.\\n\\nIn a similar vein to jailbreaks, numerous researchers have demonstrated that aligned models can easily be un-aligned\\\\citep{yang2023shadow, qi2023fine}, sometimes with as few as 100 fine-tuning examples.\\nWe view our work as adding a mechanistic understanding of such phenomena.", "subsection": []}]}', '{"id": "8", "name": "Conclusion", "content": "\\\\label{sec:conclusion}In this work we studied the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms unlearn a capability, taking DPO and toxicity as a case study.\\nFirst, we uncovered how toxicity is represented and elicited in a pre-trained language model.\\nWe find numerous vectors in MLP blocks that promote toxicity.\\nSimply subtracting these vectors from the residual stream can suppress toxic outputs.\\n\\nSecond, we applied DPO to our language model, using PPLM to carefully craft pairs of toxic and non-toxic continuations to Wikipedia prompts.\\n\\nThird, we studied how our aligned model\\\\dpo{}averts toxicity.\\nRather than removing the regions that elicit toxicity,\\\\dpo{}bypasses them by learning an\\\\emph{offset}.\\nSuch an offset is distributed amongst multiple value vectors, allowing minimal changes to every weight.\\nThis allows the model to preserve its pre-trained behavior, while averting toxic outputs, which matches the objective of the DPO loss.\\n\\nGiven this understanding, we demonstrated how to break the alignment of\\\\dpo{}, reverting it back to its toxic behavior.\\nNamely, we simply increase the regions that elicit toxicity, by scaling their corresponding key vectors.\\n\\nWe view our findings as a mechanistic case study for why aligned models can be jailbroken, and hope that this can lead to more robust alignment algorithms.\\nOur code, models, and data can be found at\\\\url{https://github.com/ajyl/dpo_toxic}.% Acknowledgements should only appear in the accepted version.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "9", "name": "Acknowledgements", "content": "We thank Ekdeep Singh Lubana for fruitful discussions, and Santiago Serra Castro for helping with figures.\\nThis work was supported via NSF under grant\\\\#2306372.%\\\\textbf{Do not} \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}, E\\\\big) = \\\\frac{\\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}+\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}}{Z\\\\big(E (\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}))} \\\\propto \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)} \\\\cdot \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}\\n\\\\end{align}where$\\\\mathbf{e}_w$is the token embedding of$w$, and$Z$is the softmax normalization factor.\\nThis indicates that when${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} > 0}$, the likelihood of$w$increases, while${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} < 0}$decreases the likelihood.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "11", "name": "Shift in Residual Streams", "content": "\\\\label{sec:appx_delta_resid}In this section we provide more examples of residual streams shifting out of toxic regions.\\nSee Figure~\\\\ref{fig:appx_pca}\\\\begin{figure*}[ht]\\n\\\\vskip 0.2in\\n\\\\begin{center}\\n\\\\centerline{\\\\includegraphics[width=\\\\columnwidth]{figures/appx/pca_layer_appx.pdf}}\\n\\\\caption{\\nShift in residual streams at layer 12, 18, and 13 (we show these three layers because $\\\\text{MLP}.\\\\mathbf{v}^{12}_{771}$, $\\\\text{MLP}.\\\\mathbf{v}^{18}_{2669}$, and $\\\\text{MLP}.\\\\mathbf{v}^{13}_{668}$ are the next three vectors with highest cosine similarity with $W_{\\\\text{Toxic}}$. 'encoding_format': 'float', 'input': ['{"id": "1", "name": "Introduction", "content": "\\\\label{sec:intro}Large language models learn surprising capabilities from pre-training on large datasets\\\\citep{gpt2FewShotLearners, chowdhery2023palm, touvron2023llama}.\\nWhile these capabilities lead to impressive achievements, they also include unwanted behaviors that can be found in large-scale web data, such as toxicity and bias\\\\citep{sheng-etal-2019-woman, gehman-etal-2020-realtoxicityprompts}.\\nAs a result, researchers have developed alignment algorithms to reduce undesirable behaviors, which often use reinforcement learning with human preferences (RLHF).\\nFor instance, proximal policy optimization (PPO,\\\\citealt{schulman2017proximal}) fits a reward model on human preference data, which is then used to fine-tune a language model, while direct preference optimization (DPO,\\\\citealt{rafailov2023direct}) by-passes the reward model and derives reward signals directly from pairwise preference data.\\n\\nWhile such algorithms can suppress undesirable behavior, our understanding of the mechanisms by which the undesirable behavior is suppressed is limited.\\nFurthermore, researchers have demonstrated that such alignments can be surprisingly easily undone\\\\citep{wallace-etal-2019-universal, zou2023universal, wei2023jailbroken, carlini2023are}.\\nWhile prior work hypothesize why jailbreaks are possible through empirical studies~\\\\citep{wei2023jailbroken}, in this work we provide a mechanistic explanation for such phenomena.\\n\\n\\nGiven the above limitations, in this work we study the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms alter a model\'s behavior.\\nResearchers have demonstrated that a deep enough understanding of a model\'s inner representations allows us to interpret how it makes decisions.\\nFor instance, various concepts such as world models, truthfulness, or even task-specific features have highly interpretable and controllable representations\\\\citep{li2023inference, todd2023function, nanda2023emergent}.\\nMotivated by such findings, we study how the representation space of language models change by comparing it before and after an alignment algorithm is applied.\\nOur work relates to that of\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}, which studies how the capabilities of a language model changes after fine-tuning on synthetic tasks.\\nUnlike this previous work, we study the change in mechanisms from a RLHF algorithm on a natural language setting.\\n\\nWe consider DPO and toxicity as a case-study of RLHF alignment algorithms.\\nWe first study how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2-medium (henceforth GPT2).\\nWe then apply DPO using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset that consists of toxic and nontoxic samples.\\nLastly, we study the mechanisms by which toxicity is no longer generated after DPO, and how those mechanisms can fail.\\n\\n\\nOur work is organized as follows: in Section~\\\\ref{sec:preliminaries}we provide the necessary preliminaries relevant to our ', 'of a language model changes after fine-tuning on synthetic tasks.\\nUnlike this previous work, we study the change in mechanisms from a RLHF algorithm on a natural language setting.\\n\\nWe consider DPO and toxicity as a case-study of RLHF alignment algorithms.\\nWe first study how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2-medium (henceforth GPT2).\\nWe then apply DPO using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset that consists of toxic and nontoxic samples.\\nLastly, we study the mechanisms by which toxicity is no longer generated after DPO, and how those mechanisms can fail.\\n\\n\\nOur work is organized as follows: in Section~\\\\ref{sec:preliminaries}we provide the necessary preliminaries relevant to our work.\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}, we demonstrate how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2.\\nWe find multiple vectors in multilayer perceptron (MLP) blocks that promote toxicity.\\nWe apply singular value decomposition (SVD) to these toxic vectors to find vectors that represent specific dimensions of toxicity in the model.\\nTo validate the role of these vectors in generating toxic outputs, we intervene with our toxic vectors and demonstrate much safer outputs.\\n\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:dpo}, we explain our procedure to apply DPO on our language models to reduce toxicity, using a carefully crafted pairwise toxicity dataset, produced by using PPLM~\\\\citep{dathathri2019plug}to generate paired toxic and non-toxic samples.\\n\\n\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:post_dpo}, we demonstrate how toxicity is no longer elicited after DPO.\\nNamely, we show that every parameter is minimally shifted, including the toxic vectors.\\nHowever, such minimal changes in weights allow the model to avert the triggering of toxic vectors.\\nPut differently, DPO\\\\emph{does not remove}the capability of generating toxic outputs, but learns an``offset\'\', distributed amongst its layers, to``bypass\'\'the regions that elicit toxicity.\\nBased on this understanding, we demonstrate the ease of re-activating these vectors to generate toxic outputs, and thus undoing the alignment learned from DPO.\\nWe view our findings as shedding light into why aligned models can be jailbroken or un-aligned.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "2", "name": "Preliminaries", "content": "\\\\label{sec:preliminaries}In this section we provide background and notations, much of which is borrowed from\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}.\\\\paragraph{Transformers, MLPs.}Transformer-based language models typically consists of embedding and unembedding layers$E, U \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{|\\\\mathcal{V}|\\\\times d}$with a series of$L$transformer layers in-between\\\\citep{attentionIsAllYouNeed}.\\nEach layer$l$consists of attention heads and a multilayer perception (MLP) layer.\\n\\nGiven an input sequence${\\\\mathbf{w} = \\\\langle w_0, ..., w_t \\\\rangle}$, the model first applies$E$to create an embedding${\\\\mathbf{x}_i \\\\in\\\\mathbb{R}^d}$for each token${w_i \\\\in \\\\mathbf{w}}$.\\nWe call${\\\\mathbf{x}_i}$the residual stream.\\n\\nThe residual stream is then updated by attention heads and MLP blocks from subsequent layers (bias terms omitted):$$\\n    \\\\mathbf{x^{\\\\ell+1}_i} = x^\\\\ell_i + \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(x^\\\\ell_i + \\\\texttt{Att}^\\\\ell(x^\\\\ell_i))\\n$$When needed, we specify the intermittent residual stream at layer$\\\\ell$(after the attention head, before the MLP) as$\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell\\\\_mid}$.\\nPer\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}, the updates to the residual stream from each MLP block can be further decomposed.\\nNamely, MLP blocks consist of two linear transformations, with point-wise activations$\\\\sigma$in-between:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp}\\n    \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(\\\\mathbf{x}^\\\\ell) = \\\\sigma\\\\left(W_K^\\\\ell\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\right) W_V^\\\\ell,\\n\\\\end{align}where$W_K^\\\\ell, W_V^\\\\ell \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d_{mlp} \\\\times d}$.\\nWe notate the$i$-th row in$W_K$as$\\\\text{MLP.}\\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}$and refer to them as key-vectors, and the$i$-th column in$W_V$,$\\\\text{MLP.}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}$, as value-vectors (we sometimes omit``$\\\\text{MLP}$\'\'and just use$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}, \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$).\\n\\nEquation~\\\\eqref{eq:mlp}indicates that\\\\emph{the output of MLP blocks is the sum of its value vectors $\\\\mathbf{v}_i$, each scaled by a coefficient value $m_i^\\\\ell$}, where$\\\\mathbf{m}^\\\\ell := \\\\sigma\\\\left(W_K^\\\\ell \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\right) \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^{d_{mlp}}$:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp_as_sum_of_value_vectors}\\n  \\\\texttt{MLP}^\\\\ell(\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}) = \\\\sum_{i=1}^{d_{mlp}} \\\\sigma(\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{k}_i^{\\\\ell}) \\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} = \\\\sum_{i=1}^{d_{mlp}} m_i^{\\\\ell} \\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}.\\n\\\\end{align}Put differently, the MLP block writes to the residual stream$d_{mlp}$times, once for each value vector.\\nWe call each of these updates a\\\\emph{sub-update}.\\n\\nAll of our experiments are conducted with GPT2-medium, which has$L = 24$,$d = 1024$, and$d_{mlp} = 4096$.\\\\paragraph{Interpreting Value Vectors in Vocabulary Space.}\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that for each sub-update, each value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$either promotes or suppresses the likelihood of a token$w$from being generated:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:value_vecs_promote}\\n  p&\\\\big(w \\\\;|\\\\; \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}, E\\\\big) &\\\\propto \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)} \\\\cdot \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}\\n  \\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}where$\\\\mathbf{e}_w$is the embedding of$w$.\\nThis indicates that when${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} > 0}$, the likelihood of$w$increases, while${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} < 0}$decreases the likelihood.\\\\footnote{See Appendix for derivation.}Further note that this dot product can be further decomposed.\\nNamely,$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is a``static\'\'value that does not depend on the input: only when$\\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is scaled by$m_i$(which is determined by the its corresponding key vector,$\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell$, and the residual stream$\\\\textbf{x}$) do we see the impact of the ', 'in Vocabulary Space.}\\\\citet{geva-etal-2022-transformer}demonstrate that for each sub-update, each value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$either promotes or suppresses the likelihood of a token$w$from being generated:\\\\begin{align}\\n\\\\label{eq:value_vecs_promote}\\n  p&\\\\big(w \\\\;|\\\\; \\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell} + m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}, E\\\\big) &\\\\propto \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w\\\\cdot\\\\mathbf{x}^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)} \\\\cdot \\\\exp{\\\\big(\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\big)}\\n  \\\\nonumber\\n\\\\end{align}where$\\\\mathbf{e}_w$is the embedding of$w$.\\nThis indicates that when${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} > 0}$, the likelihood of$w$increases, while${\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot m_i^{\\\\ell}\\\\mathbf{v}_i^{\\\\ell} < 0}$decreases the likelihood.\\\\footnote{See Appendix for derivation.}Further note that this dot product can be further decomposed.\\nNamely,$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is a``static\'\'value that does not depend on the input: only when$\\\\mathbf{v}_i^\\\\ell$is scaled by$m_i$(which is with the highest dot products$\\\\mathbf{e}_w \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{v}_i$are promoted most by value vector$\\\\mathbf{v}_i$.\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}we show value vectors that "content": "\\\\label{subsec:dpo_background}DPO relies on pairwise preference data, in which given a prompt, we have a preferred (positive) continuation and a non-preferred (negative) continuation.\\nGiven each preference pair, the algorithm promotes the likelihood of the positive sample, while suppressing the likelihood of the negative sample, using the following loss term:\\\\begin{equation}\\n\\\\begin{aligned}\\n    &\\\\mathcal{L}_\\\\text{DPO} = -\\\\mathbb{E}\\\\left[\\\\log \\\\sigma \\\\left(\\\\beta \\\\log P - \\\\beta \\\\log N\\\\right)\\\\right] \\\\nonumber, \\\\\\\\\\n    &P = \\\\frac{\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}(y_+\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}{\\\\pi_{ref}(y_+\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}, N = \\\\frac{\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}(y_-\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})}{\\\\pi_{ref}(y_-\\\\mid \\\\mathbf{w})},\\n\\\\end{aligned}\\n\\\\end{equation}where$y_+$and$y_-$are preferred (nontoxic) and non-preferred (toxic) continuations of$\\\\mathbf{w}$,$\\\\pi_{ref}$is the frozen weights of the original language model, and$\\\\pi_{\\\\theta}$is the weights of the language model being updated (See\\\\citet{rafailov2023direct}for details).\\nThe algorithm promotes the likelihood of$P$, while suppressing the likelihood of$N$.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "4.2", "name": "Constructing Pairwise Toxic Data", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:construct_dpo_data}We build our pairwise toxicity dataset using PPLM\\\\citep{dathathri2019plug}.\\nPPLM is an attribute-controlled language generation technique, which attaches a simple linear attribute classification layer,$p(a|\\\\mathbf{w})$onto a language model to guide its generation.\\nDuring generation, PPLM uses the attribute classifier to compute the gradients that increases the likelihood of the language model\'s output to contain the desired attribute$a$, and shifts the activations in such direction (See\\\\citet{dathathri2019plug}for details):\\\\useshortskip$$p(y\\\\mid a) \\\\propto p(y)p(a \\\\mid y)$$To generate pairwise preference data, we use sentences from Wikitext-2\\\\citep{merity2016pointer}as prompts.\\nFor each prompt, we generate a positive sample using greedy sampling with GPT2, while using PPLM to generate negative (toxic) samples.\\nWe use our toxic probe\\\\toxicprobesymbol{}as our attribute classifier to guide towards toxic outputs.\\nWe create 24,576 pairs of toxic and nontoxic continuations.\\\\footnote{We release this data to enable further studies.}We train until validation loss converges with a patience value of 10, which occurs after approximately 6,000 sample pairs.\\nAppendix~\\\\ref{sec:hyperparams}has details for DPO and PPLM hyperparameters.\\\\begin{figure}\\n\\\\vskip "\\\\label{subsec:toxic_vectors_after_dpo}Of the toxic vectors described in Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}, note that\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}are actual weights of the model. \\nThus we high dot products to activate its corresponding value vector:\\\\begin{equation}\\n\\\\label{eq:mlp_region}\\n    \\\\gamma(\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell) := \\\\{\\\\mathbf{g} | \\\\mathbf{g} \\\\in \\\\mathbb{R}^d, \\\\sigma(\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\ell \\\\cdot \\\\mathbf{g}) > 0\\\\},\\n\\\\end{equation}where$\\\\sigma$is a non-linear activation. \\nPut differently, for all key-vector regions that Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_jailbreak}We view our work as providing a mechanistic explanation for why aligned models can be undone or jailbroken--in our experiments, the regions that previously elicited toxic behavior does not change after DPO.\\nRather,\\\\dpo{}learns minimal changes spread across layers to avoid such regions and receive its reward.\\n\\nWith such knowledge, we conjecture that more robust alignment algorithms can be designed.\\nFor instance, can we eliminate undesirable regions, as opposed to bypassing them?\\nIn scenarios like ours, in which we can identify the weights that elicit undesirable outputs, what happens if we only updated those weights in isolation?\\nSimilarly, if DPO merely learned an offset that avoids toxic regions, can we replicate this behavior by only updating the bias terms?\\n\\nAlternatively, prior to deploying language models, perhaps we can add``suppression heads\'\'--layers that suppress undesirable behavior.\\nWhat would happen if we only updated late layers (or added layers) during alignment?\\n\\nLastly, can we characterize``jailbreak-ability\'\'or``unalign-ability\'\'of aligned models, without relying on test samples?\\n\\nWe leave these questions for future work.%\\\\subsection{Characterizing Robustness}%\\\\label{subsec:measuring_alignment_robustness}%If alignment robustness is the goal, how should we characterize robustness?%The answer may depend on the attack.%To the best of our knowledge, current literature lacks a taxonomy of adversarial cases.%For instance, whether an adversary only has access to an API, or to the weights of a model can lead to different attacks.%Our safety designs may depend largely on such characteristics.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "6.2", "name": "On the Role of KL-Divergence Regularization", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:discuss_kl_div}We hypothesize that the minimal changes distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not ', 'distributed across all layers is due to the KL-divergence term that is commonly incorporated in the loss terms of RLHF algorithms.\\nNamely, the KL-divergence term discourages each weight from shifting too drastically, in order to preserve its capabilities learned during pre-training.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}fine-tunes a language model on synthetic tasks to study the changes in its mechanisms.\\nInterestingly, unlike our findings, the authors demonstrate that the model simply learns``wrappers\'\'at late layers that optimize for each task.\\n\\nWe find this difference in model training behavior interesting, and conjecture that the KL-divergence term may play a role in this difference.\\nNote that fine-tuning typically does not entail a KL-divergence term.\\nPerhaps this allows the model to make drastic and localized changes, such as in late layers, as opposed to distributed, minimal changes.%\\\\subsection{On Generalizing Findings from Our Case Study}%\\\\label{subsec:generalizing_findings_from_case_study}%We note that our findings are from a case study, on a specific language model, task, and data set.%An obvious extension would be to study a larger model.%While in principle, we do not expect the "subsection": [{"id": "7.1", "name": "Alignment Algorithms", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_alignment_algorithms}Numerous alignment algorithms have been proposed, and the choice of algorithm may largely depend on the type of data available.\\nPerhaps most commonly, human feedback data is used\\\\citep{stiennon2020learning, ouyang2022training, touvron2023llama}for methods such as PPO\\\\citep{schulman2017proximal}or DPO\\\\citep{rafailov2023direct}.\\nWhen labels for only undesirable behavior is available, algorithms like unlikelihood training\\\\citep{Welleck2020Neural}or Cringe\\\\citep{adolphs-etal-2023-cringe, xu2023some}can be used.\\nWe study DPO because it is easy to use and currently widely used.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.2", "name": "Mechanistic Interpretability", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_mech_interp}The goal of mechanistic interpretability is largely to reverse engineer model behaviors\\\\citep{olah2020zoom, elhage2021mathematical, geva-etal-2021-transformer}.\\nBy doing so, researchers have uncovered various interpretable and controllable representations, such as world models\\\\citep{li2023emergent, nanda2023emergent},``truthfulness\'\'\\\\citep{li2023inference}, knowledge\\\\citep{meng2022locating, hernandez2023linearity, burns2023discovering, geva2023dissecting}, linguistic properties\\\\citep{conneau-etal-2018-cram, tenney-etal-2019-bert}, or even tasks\\\\citep{ilharco2022editing, hendel-etal-2023-context, todd2023function}.\\n\\nRather than probing for specific representations, researchers have also characterized the representations of language models from a geometric perspective\\\\citep{park2023linear}.\\\\citet{balestriero2023characterizing}demonstrate a geometric characterization that can be used to extract feature representations that solve toxicity detection.\\n\\nSimilar to our work,\\\\citet{jain2023mechanistically}study the mechanisms in which fine-tuning on synthetic tasks alters the model\'s capabilities.\\nWe study the effects of RLHF on a more realistic, natural language setting.", "subsection": []}, {"id": "7.3", "name": "Jailbreaking Aligned Models", "content": "\\\\label{subsec:related_work_jailbreak}Researchers demonstrated that aligned models can be surprisingly easily jailbroken\\\\citep{wallace-etal-2019-universal, zou2023universal, wei2023jailbroken, carlini2023are}.\\nSuch adversarial attacks typically involve searching for prompts that can elicit previously unlearned behaviors, or even personal information\\\\citep{nasr2023scalable}.\\\\citet{carlini2023are}show that multimodal models can also be jailbroken.\\\\citet{wei2023jailbroken}provide hypotheses, backed by empirical studies, as to why language models can be jailbroken.\\n\\nIn a similar vein to jailbreaks, numerous researchers have demonstrated that aligned models can easily be un-aligned\\\\citep{yang2023shadow, qi2023fine}, sometimes with as few as 100 fine-tuning examples.\\nWe view our work as adding a mechanistic understanding of such phenomena.", "subsection": []}]}', '{"id": "8", "name": "Conclusion", "content": "\\\\label{sec:conclusion}In this work we studied the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms unlearn a capability, taking DPO and toxicity as a case study.\\nFirst, we uncovered how toxicity is represented and elicited in a pre-trained language model.\\nWe find numerous vectors in MLP blocks that promote toxicity.\\nSimply subtracting these vectors from the residual stream can suppress toxic outputs.\\n\\nSecond, we applied DPO to our language model, using PPLM to carefully craft pairs of toxic and non-toxic continuations to Wikipedia prompts.\\n\\nThird, we studied how our aligned model\\\\dpo{}averts toxicity.\\nRather than removing the regions that elicit toxicity,\\\\dpo{}bypasses them by learning an\\\\emph{offset}.\\nSuch an offset is distributed amongst multiple value vectors, allowing minimal changes to every weight.\\nThis allows the model to preserve its pre-trained behavior, while averting toxic outputs, which matches the objective of the DPO loss.\\n\\nGiven this understanding, we demonstrated how to break the alignment of\\\\dpo{}, reverting it back to its toxic behavior.\\nNamely, we simply increase the regions that elicit toxicity, by scaling their corresponding key vectors.\\n\\nWe view our findings as a mechanistic case study for why aligned models can be jailbroken, and hope that this can lead to more robust alignment algorithms.\\nOur code, models, and data can be found at\\\\url{https://github.com/ajyl/dpo_toxic}.% Acknowledgements should only appear in the accepted version.", "subsection": []}', '{"id": "9", "name": "Acknowledgements", The Toxicity Probe Vector is defined as a learned weight vector W_Toxic ∈ ℝ^d derived by training a linear probe model for binary toxicity classification, using the average residual stream (i.e., the token embeddings from the final pre-MLP layer averaged across all timesteps) extracted from a pre-trained language model. In this implementation, the probe model is trained on the Jigsaw toxic comment classification dataset, consisting of 561,808 comments labeled as toxic or non-toxic, with a 90:10 split for training and validation, and is designed to compute the probability P(Toxic | x̄^(L−1)) via a softmax function applied to the product of W_Toxic and the averaged residual stream x̄^(L−1). This learned vector, achieving an accuracy of 94% on the validation split, serves as an aggregate representation of toxicity signals within the model and is subsequently employed to measure cosine similarity with other internal representations, such as value vectors in the MLP blocks that are found to promote toxicity. The technique further involves identifying those MLP value vectors with the highest cosine similarity to W_Toxic, stacking them into an N × d matrix (with N typically set to 128), and applying singular value decomposition to obtain basis vectors that span the toxicity representation space. Additionally, analyses indicate that shifts in these value vectors, as observed in the various MLP layers, occur in the opposite direction to shifts in the residual stream, a phenomenon attributed to the sparsity of active neurons and the properties of the GeLU activation function. This detailed procedure provides a mechanistic understanding of how toxicity is encoded and manipulated within pre-trained language models.

\"subsection\": []}, {\"id\": \"3.3\", \"name\": \"Interventions Using Toxic Vectors\", \"content\": \"\\\\label{subsec:intervention}To validate the role that the toxic vectors play in eliciting toxic 2025-09-21 04:47:46,147 - llm.llm_def - DEBUG - OpenAI query completed in 8.36s Response: ```
This technique identifies internal value vectors within the MLP blocks that promote toxic outputs by first training a linear probe on a binary toxicity classification task using the Jigsaw toxic comment dataset with a 90:10 split for training and validation, where the probe is applied on the residual stream in the final layer averaged over all timesteps. The resulting Toxicity Probe Vector, which aggregates the relevant signals for toxicity, is then used to compute the cosine similarity with each MLP value vector, and those vectors that exhibit high similarity are selected as toxic vectors, with their corresponding key vectors also recorded. A set of N = 128 toxic value vectors is extracted and stacked into an N × d matrix, upon which singular value decomposition (SVD) is applied to yield decomposed singular value vectors; each singular value vector, denoted as the ith singular value vector, serves as a basis vector that spans the toxicity representation space within the model. This process ensures that the decomposed toxic vectors capture the specific dimensions and distinct contexts of toxicity present in the language model’s internal representations.

2025-09-21 04:47:56,050 - llm.llm_def - DEBUG - OpenAI query completed in 8.99s Response: ```
Toxicity Intervention via Residual Subtraction involves modifying the model’s forward pass by directly subtracting a scaled toxic vector—derived from MLP layers with high dot product correlations to toxic tokens—from the residual stream at the last layer or specific intermediary layers to suppress the activation of toxic tokens. In practice, during the forward pass the residual activation vector is updated according to the equation x^(L−1) = x^(L−1) − α · W, where W designates one of the toxic vectors identified (for example, via cosine similarity with tokens that have high toxicity associations) and α is a heuristic scalar chosen to ensure that while the toxicity score decreases, generation quality as measured by metrics such as perplexity and F1 remains comparable to the original performance. This direct intervention demonstrates the causal role of the toxic vectors in eliciting toxic outputs while preserving pre-trained language modeling behavior, and its efficacy is typically validated using toxic prompts (e.g., from the RealToxicityPrompts dataset) that initially elicit toxic responses.

The methodology leverages pairwise preference data—where a prompt is paired with a positive (non‐toxic) and a negative (toxic) continuation—to adjust the model’s parameters using a loss function that promotes the likelihood of non‐toxic generations. Importantly, DPO minimally alters individual weight parameters (ensured by KL-divergence regularization), instead learning a distributed offset in the residual stream so that toxic regions are effectively bypassed.'

1.  Technical Concept from the paper A Mechanistic Understanding of Alignment Algorithms: A Case Study on DPO  and Toxicity: 
{"name": "Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) for Toxicity Alignment", "description": "An end‐to‐end alignment procedure that applies Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) to reduce toxicity in language models. The methodology leverages pairwise preference data—where a prompt is paired with a positive (non‐toxic) and a negative (toxic) continuation—to adjust the model’s parameters using a loss function that promotes the likelihood of non‐toxic generations. Importantly, DPO minimally alters individual weight parameters (ensured by KL-divergence regularization), instead learning a distributed offset in the residual stream so that toxic regions are effectively bypassed."}

2. Relevant Excerpt of this Technique:
["{\"id\": \"4\", \"name\": \"Toxicity Alignment Using DPO\", \"content\": \"\\\\label{sec:dpo}We next describe our alignment procedure using DPO.\", \"subsection\": [{\"id\": \"4.1\", \"name\": \"Background: DPO\", \"content\": \"\\\\label{subsec:dpo_background}DPO relies on pairwise preference data, in which given a prompt, we have a preferred (positive) continuation and a non-preferred (negative) continuation.\\nGiven each preference pair, the algorithm promotes the likelihood of the positive sample, while suppressing the likelihood of the negative sample, using the following loss term:\\\\begin{equation}\\n\\\\begin{aligned}\\n    &\\\\mathcal{L}_\\\\text{DPO} = -\\\\mathbb{E}\\\\left[\\\\log \\\\sigma \\\\left(\\\\beta \\\\log P - \\\\beta \\\\log N\\\\right)\\\\right] 2025-09-21 04:48:06,339 - llm.llm_def - DEBUG - OpenAI query completed in 9.21s Response: ```
Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) for Toxicity Alignment is an end‐to‐end alignment procedure that reduces toxicity in language models by leveraging pairwise preference data where each prompt is coupled with a preferred (non‐toxic) and a non‐preferred (toxic) continuation. The method applies a loss function defined as ℒ_DPO = -𝔼[log σ(β log P - β log N)], with P = π_θ(y₊ | w) / π_ref(y₊ | w) and N = π_θ(y₋ | w) / π_ref(y₋ | w), thereby promoting the likelihood of non‐toxic outputs while suppressing toxic ones. This procedure integrates KL-divergence regularization to ensure that the individual weight parameters are minimally altered, as demonstrated by near-identical cosine similarity scores (greater than 0.99) and extremely small norm differences (typically less than 1e-5, except for the unembedding layer), effectively preserving the pre-trained language modeling behavior. Rather than removing toxic vectors present in the multilayer perceptron (MLP) blocks, DPO learns a distributed offset in the residual stream that avoids the activation regions of these toxic key vectors—defined as subspaces where the MLP key vectors activate their corresponding value vectors—thus bypassing the regions that would normally elicit toxic outputs. This alignment is achieved using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset generated, for example, by employing PPLM to produce paired toxic and non‐toxic continuations from Wikipedia prompts, resulting in thousands of sample pairs, and the learned offset mechanism explains both the effective reduction of toxicity in the model and its potential reversibility through re-scaling of the toxic key vectors.

studied the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms unlearn a capability, taking DPO and toxicity as a case study.\\nFirst, we uncovered how toxicity is represented and elicited in a pre-trained language model.\\nWe find numerous vectors in MLP blocks that promote toxicity.\\nSimply subtracting these vectors from the residual stream can suppress toxic outputs.\\n\\nSecond, we applied DPO to our language model, using PPLM to carefully craft pairs of toxic and non-toxic continuations to Wikipedia prompts.\\n\\nThird, we studied how our aligned model\\\\dpo{}averts toxicity.\\nRather than removing the regions that elicit toxicity,\\\\dpo{}bypasses them by learning an\\\\emph{offset}.\\nSuch an offset is distributed amongst multiple value vectors, allowing minimal changes to every weight.\\nThis allows the model to preserve its pre-trained behavior, while averting toxic outputs, which matches the objective of the DPO loss.\\n\\nGiven this understanding, we demonstrated how to break the alignment of\\\\dpo{}, reverting it back to its toxic behavior.\\nNamely, we simply increase the regions that elicit toxicity, by scaling their corresponding key vectors.\\n\\nWe view our findings as a mechanistic case study for why aligned models can be jailbroken, and hope that this can lead to more robust alignment algorithms.\\nOur code, models, and data can be found algorithms.\\nWe first study how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2-medium (henceforth GPT2).\\nWe then apply DPO using a carefully crafted pairwise dataset that consists of toxic and nontoxic samples.\\nLastly, we study the mechanisms by which toxicity is no longer generated after DPO, and how those mechanisms can fail.\\n\\n\\nOur work is organized as follows: in Section~\\\\ref{sec:preliminaries}we provide the necessary preliminaries relevant to our work.\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:gpt2_toxic_representation}, we demonstrate how toxicity is represented and elicited in GPT2.\\nWe find multiple vectors in multilayer perceptron (MLP) blocks that promote toxicity.\\nWe apply singular value decomposition (SVD) to these toxic vectors to find vectors that represent specific dimensions of toxicity in the model.\\nTo validate the role of these vectors in generating toxic outputs, we intervene with our toxic vectors and demonstrate much safer outputs.\\n\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:dpo}, we explain our procedure to apply DPO on our language models to reduce toxicity, using a carefully crafted pairwise toxicity dataset, produced by using PPLM~\\\\citep{dathathri2019plug}to generate paired toxic and non-toxic samples.\\n\\n\\nIn Section~\\\\ref{sec:post_dpo}, we demonstrate how toxicity is no longer elicited after DPO.\\nNamely, we show that every parameter is minimally shifted, including the toxic vectors.\\nHowever, such minimal changes in weights allow the model to avert the triggering of toxic vectors.\\nPut differently, DPO\\\\emph{does not remove}the capability of generating toxic outputs, but learns an``offset'', distributed amongst its layers, to``bypass''the regions that elicit toxicity.\\nBased on this understanding, we demonstrate the ease of re-activating these vectors to generate toxic outputs, and thus undoing the alignment learned from DPO.\\nWe view our findings as shedding light into why aligned models can be jailbroken or un-aligned.\", \"subsection\": []}"]

2025-09-21 04:48:17,763 - llm.llm_def - DEBUG - OpenAI query completed in 9.94s Response: ``` 
Pairwise Toxic Data Construction using PPLM is a method for generating a dataset of paired continuations specifically designed for Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) training. In this process, sentences from WikiText-2 serve as prompts and for each prompt a positive (preferred, non-toxic) continuation is produced via standard greedy sampling using GPT2, while a corresponding negative (non-preferred, toxic) sample is generated by employing Plug-and-Play Language Models (PPLM). PPLM, which incorporates a simple linear attribute classification layer to the language model, uses a toxicity probe as its attribute classifier; during generation, this classifier computes gradients that shift the activations in favor of outputting toxic language, thereby biasing the model toward generating toxic continuations. The construction yields a comprehensive dataset of 24,576 toxic and non-toxic continuation pairs, which are subsequently utilized in training the DPO algorithm. The DPO training, aimed at promoting the likelihood of non-toxic outputs while suppressing toxic responses, continues until validation loss convergence is observed with a patience value of ten, typically occurring after approximately 6,000 sample pairs are processed.

promote toxicity.\\nSimply subtracting these vectors from the residual stream can suppress toxic outputs.\\n\\nSecond, we applied DPO to our language model, using PPLM to carefully craft pairs of toxic and non-toxic continuations to Wikipedia prompts.\\n\\nThird, we studied how our aligned model\\\\dpo{}averts toxicity.\\nRather than removing the regions that elicit toxicity,\\\\dpo{}bypasses them by learning an\\\\emph{offset}.\\nSuch an offset is distributed amongst multiple value vectors, allowing minimal changes to every weight.\\nThis allows the model to preserve its pre-trained behavior, while averting toxic outputs, which matches the objective of the DPO loss.\\n\\nGiven this understanding, we demonstrated how to break the alignment of\\\\dpo{}, reverting it back to its toxic behavior.\\nNamely, we simply increase the regions that elicit toxicity, by scaling their corresponding key vectors.\\n\\nWe view our findings as a mechanistic case study for why aligned models can be jailbroken, and hope that this can lead to more robust alignment algorithms.\\nOur code, models, and data can be found at\\\\url{https://github.com/ajyl/dpo_toxic}.% Acknowledgements should for$\\\\\\\\text{MLP}.\\\\\\\\mathbf{k}_{\\\\\\\\text{Toxic}}$and\\\\\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}as well--toxic MLP vectors\\\\\\\\textbf{do not change}from DPO.\\\\n\\\\nPut differently, although toxicity is reduced by DPO, the ability to elicit toxicity with these value vectors still remain.\\\\nSo how is it that\\\\\\\\dpo{}averts toxic outputs?\\\\nThough its parameters have barely moved, below we show that their collective movement is enough to avoid toxic outputs.\\\\\\\\begin{figure}\\\\n\\\\\\\\vskip 0.2in\\\\n\\\\\\\\begin{center}\\\\n\\\\\\\\centerline{\\\\\\\\includegraphics[width=\\\\\\\\columnwidth]{figures/activation_drops.pdf}}\\\\n\\\\\\\\caption{Mean activations for toxic vectors before \\"\\\\\\\\dpo{\\", \\"content\\": \\"\\\\\\\\label{subsec:avoiding_toxic_regions}In simplest terms, we observe a drop in activations for the toxic vectors\\\\\\\\toxicmlpsymbol{}in\\\\\\\\dpo{}.\\\\nNamely, using the same 1,199 prompts from\\\\\\\\textsc{RealToxicityPrompts}, we generate 20 tokens and measure the mean activations$m_i$, by\\\\\\\\citet{balestriero2023characterizing}, we visualize this drop in activations with what we call``MLP activation regions\'\'.\\\\nAn activation region of a key vector is simply a\\\\\\\\emph{subspace}within the model\'s hidden space in which its vectors have high dot products to activate its corresponding value vector:\\\\\\\\begin{equation}\\\\n\\\\\\\\label{eq:mlp_region}\\\\n    \\\\\\\\gamma(\\\\\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\\\\\ell) := \\\\\\\\{\\\\\\\\mathbf{g} | \\\\\\\\mathbf{g} \\\\\\\\in \\\\\\\\mathbb{R}^d, \\\\\\\\sigma(\\\\\\\\mathbf{k}_i^\\\\\\\\ell \\\\\\\\cdot \\\\\\\\mathbf{g}) > 0\\\\\\\\},\\\\n\\\\\\\\end{equation}where$\\\\\\\\sigma$is a non-linear activation. \\\\nPut differently, for all key-vector regions that the residual stream``passes through\'\', their corresponding value-vectors are activated, scaled, and added into the residual stream.\\\\n\\\\nWe view the drop in activations as a shift in\\\\\\\\dpo{}\'s residual stream to avert the regions of toxic MLP vectors,$\\\\\\\\gamma(\\\\\\\\text{MLP.}\\\\\\\\mathbf{k}_{\\\\\\\\text{Toxic}})$.\\\\nSee \\"content\\": \\"\\\\\\\\label{sec:conclusion}In this work we studied the mechanisms by which alignment algorithms unlearn a capability, taking DPO and toxicity as a case study.\\\\nFirst, we uncovered how toxicity is represented and elicited in a pre-trained language model.\\\\nWe find numerous vectors in MLP blocks that promote toxicity.\\\\nSimply subtracting these vectors from the residual stream can suppress toxic outputs.\\\\n\\\\nSecond, we applied DPO to our language model, using PPLM to carefully craft pairs of toxic and non-toxic continuations to Wikipedia prompts.\\\\n\\\\nThird, we studied how our aligned model\\\\\\\\dpo{}averts toxicity.\\\\nRather than removing the regions that elicit toxicity,\\\\\\\\dpo{}bypasses them by learning an\\\\\\\\emph{offset}.\\\\nSuch an offset is distributed amongst multiple value vectors, allowing minimal changes to every weight.\\\\nThis allows the model to preserve its pre-trained behavior, while averting toxic outputs, which matches the objective of the DPO loss.\\\\n\\\\nGiven this understanding, we demonstrated how to break the alignment of\\\\\\\\dpo{}, reverting it back to its toxic behavior.\\\\nNamely, we simply increase the regions that elicit toxicity, by scaling their corresponding key vectors.\\\\n\\\\nWe view our findings as a mechanistic case study for why aligned models "Reactivation of Toxicity via Scaling Toxic Key Vectors" is a technique that demonstrates the vulnerability of the apparent alignment in language models to being undone, showing that the alignment achieved by methods such as DPO can be reversed by mechanically reactivating toxic behaviors already embedded in the model. In this approach, the key vectors associated with toxic MLP components—specifically those selected as the seven vectors with the highest cosine similarity to a toxic probe vector (W_Toxic)—are scaled by a factor of 10, thereby enlarging their activation regions, γ(MLP.k_Toxic). This scaling causes the residual stream to pass through these expanded toxic regions, which in turn triggers the toxic value vectors that had been previously suppressed by an offset learned by DPO. The reactivation of these vectors reverts the model back to its pre-aligned toxic behavior, a result that is observed without a detrimental impact on perplexity, since the intervention manipulates the key vectors rather than directly altering the residual stream. This precise mechanistic intervention provides a detailed explanation for why aligned models can be easily jailbroken, as the toxic vectors—although rendered inactive during aligned operation—remain intact within the model and can be reactivated by simply scaling their corresponding key vectors.

The Pairwise Toxicity Dataset consists of preference pairs designed for training alignment algorithms by employing Direct Preference Optimization (DPO), where each entry is constructed from a neutral prompt drawn from Wikitext-2, a benign continuation generated using greedy sampling with GPT2, and a toxic continuation produced by guiding the language model with a toxicity classifier via PPLM, which applies a simple linear attribute classification layer to shift activations towards generating toxic outputs. The dataset is structured in a “chosen” (non-toxic) versus “rejected” (toxic) format that directly aligns with the DPO loss term, promoting the likelihood of the positive sample while suppressing that of the negative sample. In constructing this dataset, prompts from Wikitext-2 are used to generate a positive sample via standard generation and a negative sample using PPLM, resulting in 24,576 toxic and nontoxic continuation pairs. Training is conducted until the validation loss converges with a patience value of 10, which occurs after approximately 6,000 sample pairs, ensuring that the dataset and the resulting DPO alignment process adhere strictly to the experimental design detailed in the provided excerpts.
